BRAZIL
SECOND PROGRAMMATIC FISCAL REFORM LOAN
LOAN AND PROGRAM SUMMARY
Borrower:              Federative Republic of Brazil
Implementing           Ministry of Finance and Ministry of Planning and Budget
Agencies:
Poverty Category:      NA
Amount:                US$404.04 million (Single Tranche)
Terms:                 Fixed-spread loan in US dollars, with a repayment period of 10 years
including five years of grace.
Commitment Fee:        0.85% per annum during the first four years and 0.75% per annum
thereafter on undisbursed loan amounts, beginning to accrue sixty
days after the loan agreement is signed.
Front-End Fee:         1% of the loan amount, to be financed under the loan.
Objective:             The objectives of the government's fiscal reform program are to
reduce Brazil's remaining macroeconomic vulnerabilities, and thus
promote sustainable growth and poverty reduction. Specifically, the
proposed loan supports a set of policy measures aimed at (i)
institutionalizing responsible fiscal policy and nsk management and
(ii) initiation of reform actions to reduce the tax system's
distortionary effects on the national economy.  The measures
supported by the loan are expected to contribute to poverty reduction
by creating macroeconomic stability and a favorable investment
climate for growth. Expected results, by the end of the program in
2006, include:
*  a fiscal framework and hence an economy more resilient to
shocks:
*  reduction of the public debt/GDP ratio to around 50% with a
declining trend;
*  reduction of the risks inherent in the public debt composition;
*  reduction in the weight of distortionary cascading and payroll
taxes in the federal tax structure; and
*  elimination of inefficient fiscal competition among states, and
thus increase in their revenue collection capacity measured in
terms of the share of own taxes in the states' net current
revenues (which include constitutional transfers from the
federal government).
The broader programmatic framework for fiscal reform agenda, of
which the proposed loan forms a part, would address another related
goal, namely more equitable and efficient public expenditure.
iv



Separate programmatic loans will be prepared over the next several
years to support additional advances within the overall program.
Description:          Brazil's new Government, elected with a broad popular mandate, is
focused on social justice, while at the same time committed to
maintaining economic stability and pursuing key structural reforms.
Brazil, today, has a historic opportunity to overcome longstanding
social problems and economic vulnerability and deliver the promise
of a better quality of life for all its citizens
This opportunity comes at a time of an unusually adverse external
environment, which poses grave economic risks.   The new
government needs to quickly build a record of credibility that will
enable Brazil to improve its access to international markets.  Many
of the reforms necessary to ensure sustainable growth require difficult
decisions and compromises.  The new government is uniquely
positioned to enact these difficult reforms. President Lula is enjoying
a period of strong popularity and legitimacy, which is allowing his
administration to adopt hard economic measures and to propose and
openly discuss deep reforms. The establishment of the Council for
Economic and Social Development as a forum to discuss the
structural  reforms  between  government  and  civil society
representatives, and the two meetings of President Lula with the 27
state governors represented initiatives without precedent to build
political and social support needed for the structural reforms. After
extensive consultations, the Government has now sent to Congress
sound proposals on Social Security and Tax Reforms, which enjoy
broad societal support including from the 27 governors and the
Council.
The Government's ambitious fiscal reform agenda addresses four
broad objectives: (i) institutionalization of responsible fiscal policy
and risk management; (ii) improvements in efficiency in the revenue
structure and further strengthening of the tax administration; (iii)
efficient and equitable public expenditures geared toward provision of
public goods for poverty reduction and growth promotion; and (iv) a
more equitable and sustainable social security system.
The proposed loan addresses two of the four broad objectives of fiscal
reform mentioned above: (i) institutionalization of responsible and
credible fiscal policy and risk management; and (ii) improvements in
efficiency in the revenue structure and further strengthening of the tax
administration. Within the context of the first broad objective, the
loan would support: (a) strengthening of macro-fiscal planning; (b)
effective control of sub-national debts and sub-national fiscal
discipline; (c) improvements in fiscal transparency and reporting; (d)
capacity building in public debt management and its further
institutionalization; and (e) market development for government
securities. With respect to the second broad objective, the loan would
support a tax reform to reduce distortionary effects.
v



Regarding the first major area of the loan, namely institutionalization
of responsible and credible fiscal policy and risk management, the
following are the specific objectives and measures that the loan would
support in each sub-component:
* Strengthening of macro-fiscal planning: The overall objective
is to reduce volatility and uncertainty arising from fiscal policy
decisions. The Government has adopted a credible medium-term
fiscal framework.  Credibility has been enhanced by the
achievements of the announced primary surplus targets in four
consecutive years. The Government has begun to institutionalize
this credibility by improving the quality of its medium-term fiscal
projection and planning, primarily through the LDO Annex on
Fiscal Targets, in which it- has announced stringent primary
surplus targets for the three following years. The announced
target of 4.25 percent of GDP for 2004-06, higher than the target
agreed in the August 2002 IMF Agreement, show the
government's strong commitment to prudent fiscal policies.
Furthermore, the government is considering the adoption of a
fiscal framework that sets the primary balance as a target that
depends on the level of economic activity. This is a welcome
advance in institutionalization, especially if it is framed within a
medium term program of stabilizing and reducing indebtedness of
the government. The performance indicator of this area is the
achievement of a debt to GDP ratio of 50 percent by 2006 with a
declining trend and the achievement of the targeted surplus levels
for the 2003-2006 period.
* Effective control of sub-national debts and sub-national fiscal
discipline: The overall objective is to maintain the fiscal effort
undertaken by sub-national governments and to promote more
broad-based compliance with the LRF. The proposed loan would
support (i) the passage of the Senate Resolutions 40 and 43 to
regulate sub-national indebtedness as required by the LRF, (ii) the
National Monetary Council Resolutions 2827 and 2920 to
regulate the financial sector's credit supply to the public sector, as
well as (iii) the impressive turnaround in sub-national fiscal
behavior as evidenced in the effective compliance with key
provisions of the LRF - the personnel expenditure/net current
revenue (RCL) ratio below 60% and the debt stockIRCL of 2.0 or
less for states and 1.2 or less for municipalities.  These
resolutions have created a robust regulatory environment to
minimize the risk of excessive sub-national borrowing in the
future, while the actual performance by the majority of the sub-
national governments show that these legal provisions have been
effectively complied with. The key performance indicator in this
area is the aggregate fiscal performance of states and
municipalities with respect to the above-mentioned key LRF
parameters.
* Improvements in fiscal transDarency and reporting: The
overall objective is to sustain the marked improvements in fiscal
vi



transparency and reporting achieved since the passage of the
LRF. Notable recent achievements include the consolidation of
public sector accounts from the Union, all the state governments,
and a vast majority of the municipal governments. With respect
to the problem of contingent liabilities (so-called skeletons), the
recognition of which has contributed to 25% of the increase of
federal debt between 1994 and 2001, the Government formally
adopted a transparent method of discussing fiscal risks in an LDO
Annex. The Annex identifies multiple sources of fiscal risks and
assesses sensitivities of the budget and the public debt to different
scenarios. With this new practice, the Government achieved
substantial transparency, a necessary first step for effective
management of contingent fiscal risks. The issuance of high
quality Annex on Fiscal Risks in the LDO is a concrete action the
proposed loan would support.
o  Capacity building and strelngthenina of internal governance
arrangements for public debt management:      Substantial
progress was achieved in the institutional development of the
middle office and the Annual Borrowing Plans. The STN has
already adopted its own code of conduct in line with the
international best practices. The overall objective of this set of
measures is to create the governance structure necessary to
implement the benchmark developed by the STN Middle Office.
STN is in the process of centralizing debt management (domestic
and external) functions, to make debt managers accountable for
public debt risk levels, and has already consolidated the domestic
debt operations under its responsibility. The performance
indicator of achievements of this objective would be the debt
targets as set in the Annual Borrowing Plans for the period 2003-
2006. By meeting the Annual Borrowing Plan targets, Brazil will
gradually reduce over time the rollover risk of its debt, as well as
its exposure to exchange rate and interest rate risks.
o  Market development for government securities: The overall
objective here consists of developing a domestic market for
different types of securities, to permit the implementation of the
associated benchmark (i.e., possibility of issuing long term
securities, fixed rate nominal debt, etc.). The proposed operation
supports actions both on the supply and demand sides of
government securities. On the supply side, the government seeks
to design and implement a domestic issuance strategy, and has
advanced in this direction by reducing the fragmentation (number
and types of securities and issues).  On the demand side,
authorities aim at diversifying the investor base, to reduce
concentration risk and funding costs.
Regarding the second broad objective of the loan, namely the
improvements in efficiency and equity and in the revenue structure
the following are the measures that the loan would support.
vii



*  Initiation of a tax reform to imDrove efficiencv: The loan
would support recent advances in tax policy changes. In the last
two years the political climate surrounding the issue of tax reform
has changed dramatically. In 2002, the previous government
issued Presidential decree MP-66 (Medida Provis6ria, MP-66
later converted into Law 10.637) and initiated a modest step to
transform the small turnover PIS/PASEP into a value-added tax
base. The Law 10.637 also established similar reforms for the
larger COFINS cumulative tax for 2004. With the arrival of the
new administration which advocated a major reshuffling of the
tax system, tax reform has become one of two major fiscal
structural reform agenda being vigorously debated in the country.
The basic guidelines of the Tax Reform that the government has
submitted to Congress as a constitutional amendment proposal
have wide agreement among government and key stakeholders,
namely (i) simplification of the tax system; (ii) progressive
elimination of indirect cumulative taxation; and (iii) the
harmonization of state value-added taxation systems (ICMS).
The main points of this proposal are:
*  Progressively convert the PIS/PASEP and COFINS from
turnover tax bases to value added tax bases;
*  Leave the ICMS to the states but create national uniformity
legislation to avoid fiscal war among states;
*  Allow flexibility to reduce the CPMF rate from 0.38% to
0.08% maintaining it as an anti-evasion tool through the
cross-checking of bank information and the income tax
declarations (the plan to reduce this tax is contingent on
finding alternative deficit-neutral options so that fiscal
sustainability is not jeopardized); and
*  Increase the progressivity of the state inheritance tax.
The tax reform proposal is expected to be revenue neutral in the
medium term, while in the longer run the efficiency gains derived
from a less complex system and the end of fiscal wars among states
will lead to a less burdensome tax structure that will be conducive to
growth. In particular, in the short run to compensate for the tax base
reduction implicit in switching from a turnover-based PIS-PASEP to
a value added tax, the rate was increased from 0.65% to 1.65%.
Similarly, the rate of the COFINS tax, another turnover-based tax
which will be converted to a value-added tax, is expected to be
increased from the current basic rate of 3% to maintain revenue
neutrality. The CPMF reduction will begin to be implemented, only
when deficit-neutral options are identified. For all of the above, the
tax reform proposal is considered compatible with the medium-term
objective of ensuring debt sustainability.
Justifications for the proposed loan rest on three sets of
considerations.  First, as described above, there has been good
progress in important institutional  and  structural  reforms.
viii



Maintenance of a credible fiscal policy and a strong record of
compliance with the Law of Fiscal Responsibility at all levels of the
federal system are among the strongest evidence of improved fiscal
performance. Second, and beyond the specific measures taken and
supported by this loan, the responsible policy stance taken by the new
Government more broadly provides a very favorable context for the
proposed operation, including progress in social security reform,
responsible monetary policies, as well as decisive policies to address
poverty and inequality directly. These developments show that a
broad consensus on the basic elements of sound economic and social
policies has evolved, and would permit implementation of consistent
policies in the medium and long run. Third, despite the positive
turnaround, Brazil still finds itself in a fragile position vis-a-vis
international financial markets. Bank financial assistance in support
of fiscal reform could both send a positive signal to the market as a
validation of the Government's efforts and achievements so far, and
also address Brazil's external financing needs.
Beinefits:             The ultimate benefits of the supported reforms will be reduced
macroeconomic vulnerability, resulting in sustained growth and
poverty reduction. The policy measures supported by this loan are
intended to improve the quality of fiscal adjustment through further
institutionalization of the fiscal decision making process (setting
primary balance surpluses, establishing benchmarks for debt
composition). With the institutionalization proposal, the volatility
and uncertainty arising from fiscal policy will be mitigated. The loan
also supports a tax reform oriented at obtaining a less complex tax
system, which will reduce the burden of the public sector on private
activity and hence will contribute towards a more favorable
investment climate. By reducing uncertainty and reducing the burden
of a complicated tax structure, there will be a more favorable
environment for growth.
Risks:                 Slippage of the proposed reforms: In the recent past, tax and some
social security reform proposals faced strong political opposition and
stalled in Congress. A similar risk cannot be ignored with respect to
the latest Government reform proposals. A major setback in the
reform process or significant delay in approval would undermine
confidence in the ability of the government to deliver the promised
results. This lack of confidence could affect the public's perception of
regime sustainability. However, the extensive consensus-building
process that preceded the Government's submission of the reform bill
to Congress as well as the explicit endorsement the Government has
received from the state governors augur well for the reform's
prospect. Based on these considerations, as well as the importance of
the proposed reform, the risk is considered acceptable.
Negative external shock. Negative shocks arising from international
capital markets could lead to a sudden stop of capital flows, which
would, in turn, give rise to a currency depreciation. Though the
ix



central bank has a track record of letting the floating exchange rate
operate, the current debt composition biased towards dollar-indexed
debt would revive concerns about debt sustainability. The country's
track record of fiscal adjustment in the face of external shocks tends
to make this risk acceptable.
Financing Plan:       Not Applicable

inputs from Augusto de la Torre, Thomas Glaessner, Jeppe Ladekarl (secondary debt market), Antonio

I. INTRODUCTION
1.    President Luiz Inacio Lula da Silva was elected with a strong mandate of 61% of valid
votes in October 2002 on a platform that emphasized poverty reduction and growth. He also
committed himself to a responsible economic program, including tight fiscal policy to stabilize
public debt, inflation targeting, and the honoring of all debt contracts. The transition period
following the elections was characterized by a high degree of goodwill and cooperation between
the old and new administrations. President Lula took office on January 1, 2003.
2.    Brazil's new Government is focused on social justice, while at the same time committed
to maintaining economic stability and pursuing key structural reforms. Brazil, today, has a
historic opportunity to overcome longstanding social problems and economic vulnerability and
deliver the promise of a better quality of life for all its citizens. The new Government has
embarked on a new and ambitious social agenda, including two new flagship programs, Zero
Hunger and First Employment, intended to address hunger and misery and generate employment
for youth, respectively. The Government has also initiated serious attempts to re-organize the
social programs toward a better articulated social protection policy based on robust program
evaluations. Improving the quality of public spending is a prime concern for the Government
given its genuine commitment to social development and clear recognition of the prevailing
fiscal constraints.
3.    Brazil's unique opportunity to address long-standing social and economic issues comes at
a time of an unusually adverse external environment, which poses grave economic risks. The
Government is quickly building a record of credibility by making prudent choices regarding
fiscal and monetary policies and by aggressively pursuing a long-standing structural reform
agenda. Successful pursuit of these policies and reforms will enable Brazil to improve its access
to international markets.
4.    Many of the reforms necessary to ensure sustainable growth require difficult
compromises. Yet the new government is uniquely positioned to enact these difficult reforms.
President Lula has demonstrated the willingness to pursue sound macroeconomic policies and to
pursue difficult reforms. He is enjoying a period of strong popularity which is allowing his
administration to adopt hard economic measures and to propose and openly discuss deep
reforms. The establishment of the Council for Economic and Social Development as a forum to
discuss the structural reforms between government and civil society representatives, and the two
meetings of President Lula with the 27 state governors, represented initiatives without precedent
to build political and social support needed for the structural reforms.  After extensive
consultations, the Government has sent to Congress consensual proposals on Social Security and
Tax Reforms, which are supported by the 27 governors and the Council.
5.    Among the most innovating aspects of the new Government's governing style is its
serious commitment to broad consultations based on the principles of transparency and
participation. The above-mentioned Council and the consultations with state governors are only
the most visible of the new administration's participatory approach to governance. President
Lula's Workers' Party was the originator of the famous participatory budgeting at the municipal
level, and now his Government is trying to scale up this approach at the national level. For
I



example, the Government is initiating a participatory process for elaborating the next four-year
plan (PPA) that will be the basis of the public expenditure program for 2004-2007.
6.    Higher, sustainable growth and lasting poverty reduction are both key objectives of the
Lula administration. The Government recognizes that neither is possible without successful
structural reforms, especially in the fiscal area. If the new administration's drive for reform is
not provided the necessary support at this juncture, an opportunity to introduce lasting changes in
Brazil, benefiting current as well as future generations, may be lost. The broad programs and
policies presented by the Government are consistent with the policy matrix in this document, and
will be the basis for deepening Bank assistance and policy dialogue regarding these issues.
7.    Within this general context, the Government has requested an adjustment operation from
the Bank to support fiscal reform. In response to this request, this project document proposes a
second Programmatic Fiscal Reform Loan (PFRL II). The first loan (Programmatic Fiscal
Reform Structural Adjustment Loan, or PFRSAL) was approved by the Board in January 2001 in
support of the progress to date in fiscal management (e.g., passage of the landmark Law of Fiscal
Responsibility), public expenditure management (e.g., issuance of the multi-year expenditure
plan, PPA, with an innovative results-oriented framework), and public debt management (e.g.,
initiation of the reform to debt management governance arrangement and a Bank-supported
program of capacity building). Consistent with the CAS objective of supporting "improvements
in fiscal institutions and public sector management for better fiscal performance and incentives
for fiscal discipline,"' the proposed second Loan would support improvements in Brazil's fiscal
policy and management in the last two years within a broader medium-term program of
reform/policy agenda.. Although progress in measures supported by PFRSAL has not been
uniform across different areas, there have been notable advances toward institutionalizing
responsible fiscal management and steady improvements in debt management capacity building.
In areas where the progress has been more limited, namely public expenditure management, the
Bank will continue policy dialogue and technical assistance, and once a critical mass of
significant actions has been taken, a separate loan will be prepared to support such additional
achievements.
8.    The overall objective of the Government's fiscal reform agenda is to create a favorable
investment climate and an environment conducive to growth through a public sector adjustment
of good quality - a necessary condition to ensure overall sustainability - and to strengthen its
capacity for well-targeted intervention for poverty reduction. The overarching reform program
of the Government is composed of four main pillars: (i) institutionalization of responsible fiscal
policy and risk management; (ii) progress toward a more efficient tax system; (iii) more
equitable and efficient public expenditure; and (iv) a more equitable and sustainable social
security system.
9.    This second loan of the program seeks to underpin (i) institutionalization of responsible
fiscal policy and risk management; and (ii) reform of the tax system to reduce its distortionary
effects on the economy and to further strengthen tax administration. To these ends, the loan
would include advances achieved in policy measures in the following technical areas: (i) federal
macro-fiscal planning; (ii) effective control of sub-national debts and sub-national fiscal
'CAS, March 6, 2000; Report No. 20160-BR, p 31
2



discipline; (iii) improvements in fiscal transparency and reporting and in management of
contingent liabilities; (iv) capacity building in public -debt management and its
institutionalization; (v) market development for the sale of government securities; and (vi)
undertaking of a tax reform program.
II. GROWTH, POVERTY REDUCTION AND FISCAL REFORM
A.    MACROECONOMIC CONTEXT
10.   Brazil engaged in a process of far-reaching economic reform throughout the nineties with
liberalization of trade and price controls and privatization of public enterprises. In 1994, the
government introduced the Real plan, stabilizing inflation at internationally comparable levels;
low rates of inflation have continued till today. Until 1998, however, fiscal reform lagged
behind reform in other areas. After the end of the inflationary period in 1994, Brazilian fiscal
accounts deteriorated progressively. The deteriorating fiscal accounts and the tight monetary
policy associated with use of the exchange rate as a nominal anchor led to rising external and
public debt; eventually, in a climate where financial crises in Asia and Russia were having
contagion effects, significant capital outflow occurred from the country, and real interest rates
rose significantly. Finally, in 1999, Brazil was forced to abandon its crawling peg exchange rate
regime in favor of a flexible exchange rate with an inflation-targeting framework as the new
nominal anchor.
11.   Since then, the country initiated a significant fiscal adjustment. In October 1998, the
government launched the Fiscal Stability Program to tackle the fiscal imbalance.  The
government set and ac,hieved stringent targets for the primary fiscal surplus; the public sector
primary surplus (as a percentage of GDP) was 3.25% in 1999, 3.5% in 2000, 3.7% in 2001 and
3.9% in 2002. The government also implemented a robust sub-national fiscal adjustment
program through a series of legal measures including the state debt refinancing program (Law
9496/1997) and the landmark Law of Fiscal Responsibility enacted in May 2000.
12.   The high and sustained primary surpluses were expected to control debt accumulation
and, together with the new fiscal control measures, inspire investor confidence, bringing the real
interest rate down fairly rapidly. The fiscal outlook began to improve in 2000, only to turn
negative again in 2001-2002 largely because of the shocks including (i) the domestic energy
shock in 2001; (ii) the uncertainty and risk aversion arising from the corporate corruption
scandals in developed markets and the economic crisis in Argentina; (iii) the oil price shock
resulting from the international political situation; (iv) the growth slowdown in the United States
and Europe, and (v) the uncertainty surrounding the 2002 presidential election. As a result, the
debt/GDP ratio actually worsened from 49.3% of GDP in 2000 to 57% of GDP at the end of
2002.

13.    In 2002, Brazil managed to achieve a rapid adjustment of its external accounts. The
current account deficit fell from 4.5% of GDP during 1999 to 2001 to 1.7% of GDP in 2002.
However, because of large external financing requirements (Table 2), the economy remained
vulnerable to short-term capital market flows. This exposure adversely affected domestic
interest rates and exchange rate behavior, as well as perceptions of public sector solvency, since
much of the public debt was linked to the dollar or the short-term interest rate, or short-term in
nature.

14.   In an effort to calm the market's nervousness preceding the 2002 election, the
government entered into a $30 billion stand-by arrangement with the IMF in August 2002, which
upon its approval on September 6, 2002, made US$6 billion available during 2002, and another
US$24 billion for 2003, conditional on continued implementation of the program agreed on. The
standby arrangement is based on the maintenance of a 3.75 percent of GDP primary surplus
target for 2003 (or higher if required to stabilize the public debt to GDP ratio), and the
continuation of the inflation-targeting regime.
15.   The new administration arrived in the context described above.  The market was
concerned about Brazil's debt sustainability, high domestic debt and interest rates, and
inflationary pressures. In response to market expectations, President-elect Lula appointed a
competent economic team; the inauguration speeches by President Lula, Finance Minister
Palocci and Central Bank President Meirelles were pivotal to confirming the administration's
commitment to sound economic management. These speeches underscored the large social
inequities and tough challenges ahead; an earnest promise was made to tackle them while
maintaining fiscal responsibility.
16.   These speeches were quickly followed by concrete fiscal adjustment measures that sent
strong signals of the government's commitment to fiscal responsibility. First, in early February,
the Government announced a new primary surplus target of 4.25% of GDP, well above the level
previously agreed in the IMF Program, and higher than any previous primary surplus achieved.
Second, to make this primary surplus target materialize, the Government announced drastic cuts
in the spending limits authorized in the annual budget, while largely protecting social
expenditures. In March 2003, the IMF successfully completed a second program review and
disbursed another US$4.1 billion based on Brazil's satisfactory compliance with the program
targets. The Government's commitment to tight fiscal policy has since been formalized in the
draft Budget Guidelines Law (LDO) submitted to Congress on April 15, 2003. The LDO
establishes medium-term primary surplus targets at 4.25% of GDP for the entire period 2003-
2006.

17.   The Lula administration has been equally aggressive in pursuing structural reforms
deemed necessary for restoring credibility and sustainability in Brazil's economic management.
On April 2, 2003, the proposed Amendment 53 of the Constitutional Article 192 was voted in the
Lower House, and approved by a large majority. Once approved by both houses, the measure
would pave the way for a regular law to establish the Central Bank's operational autonomy,
which would significantly improve the overall credibility of macroeconomic management in
Brazil, and would likewise contribute to lowering the interest rate. Two other major reforms, tax
reform and social security reform, are being actively debated nation-wide, and the Government
already presented reform proposals to Congress on April 30, following broad consultations with
key stakeholders. Although these difficult reforms cannot be expected to proceed without any
opposition, the broad contours of the reform proposals have received strong endorsements in the
so-called "Letter from Brasilia" signed by the 27 state governors, who are key political
stakeholders in Brazil's federal system (Annex 3).
18.   Markets have reacted very positively to the first months under the new Government and
the perceived consistency of the macroeconomic program, as reflected in the projections of main
macroeconomic indicators (see Table 1). As of early May 2003, the exchange rate has
appreciated to below R$3.00 (from R$4 in September 2002) and sovereign bond spreads have
narrowed to below 800bps (from 2500bps in September 2002). The positive reaction of
international markets to the strong election results and the post-election statements by the
incoming president, coupled with the impact of the IMF agreement, have allowed some
breathing room for the new government to establish a track record of responsible economic
management, and to conceive and initiate its agenda for growth and poverty reduction.
19.   The medium-term growth scenario shows output growth increasing gradually and
stabilizing at around 4 percent per year. In 2002, Brazil's trade surplus rapidly increased to $12
billion, mostly as the result of import compression. The growth rate of exports was surprisingly
low in 2002, despite the unexpected depreciation.  Although quantum indices showed a
significant improvement, low export prices more than compensated for this positive trend.
During 2003, exports are expected to respond favorably to (i) the more stable currency at a
depreciated level; and (ii) a reduction in uncertainty about international conflicts and oil prices.
The export growth rate is expected to stabilize at around 4 to 5 percent in the medium term.
20.   The rapid fall in the current account deficit is not expected to persist. As growth begins
to pick up, the external balance would decrease slightly and stabilize at a moderate current
account deficit of between 2 and 3 percent of GDP. Though the financing requirement levels are
high in absolute terms, and in relation to international reserves, the floating exchange rate system
has been shown to operate well, mitigating the risk of an attack against the currency. Our
projections do not anticipate major external financing problems provided that the proposed
reform agenda is pursued in conjunction with the serious macroeconomic management currently
being implemented. However, if the refortn agenda suffers a major setback, the sizable
financing requirements would put pressure on the exchange rate and sovereign spreads, reversing
the gains achieved in the recent past.
21.   Continued stability of the Brazilian economy will depend critically on the Government's
ability to make advances with the other announced structural reform measures and on external
market developments. If the early actions on responsible economic management are followed
6



through with the implementation of a credible medium-term fiscal program (as already
announced in the draft LDO for 2004) and structural reforms required to sustain fiscal
adjustment, further market improvements would allow Brazil to enter a virtuous cycle of
improving debt and fiscal indicators, reinvigorating its investment climate, emphasizing
productivity improvements and in turn generating renewed economic growth. This would, in
turn, lead to a lasting reduction in vulnerability. In such a scenario, according to our projections,
output growth in the medium term is expected to increase gradually and stabilize at around 4
percent per year, with steady improvements also expected for other economic variables.
22.   Despite the considerable fiscal and external adjustments in recent years, it will take time
to reduce the remaining vulnerabilities. Substantial macroeconomic risks will remain for some
time. Global risk aversion continues to hamper the return of capital flows and foreign direct
investment to the country. Thus the external vulnerability continues to be a concern. The
experience from the recent past has shown the difficulties of a reduced access to external
markets. The depreciation of the real, higher inflation, an interruption in the declining trend of
domestic interest rates, and lower growth generated a perverse public debt dynamic that further
worsened access to the international market.
23.   On the internal side the main risks arise from the high level of inflation and from
uncertainty about the fate of the Government's reform proposals. High inflation sparks inflation
expectations, giving rise to high interest rates. Unless inflation expectations return to single digit
levels and the Central Bank regains its anti-inflation credibility, it will be difficult to reduce
interest rates. Without lower interest rates growth prospects will be dim. On the political front,
until the Government's proposals are actually approved in Congress, uncertainty will remain. In
case the reforms are not approved, or significantly diluted in their content, the market
perceptions of Brazil's macroeconomic fundamentals are likely to turn negative. Although the
prospects for passage of the reform proposals are better than ever in recent years because of the
Government's concerted efforts to build political support prior to presenting the proposals, the
risk of reform slippage cannot be discarded.
B.    GROWTH, POVERTY REDUCTION AND FISCAL ADJUSTMENT: MOTIVATION FOR THE
FISCAL REFORM AGENDA
24.   Economic stabilization in the early nineties achieved a significant reduction in poverty;
the percentage of the population below the poverty line fell from 45.3% in 1988 to 33.5% in
1996, after which it has remained stable.2 But most of this poverty reduction can be explained
by economic growth and the once-and-for-all effect of the price stabilization program in 1994.3
In the period since 1996, poverty reduction has stagnated, largely due to a tapering down of
economic growth since then, and to the adverse macroeconomic conditions and increased
volatility since 1998. To be able to continue poverty reduction in the near future and in the long
term, it is vital for Brazil to: (i) continue on a path of economic growth that is steady, sustainable
and equitable; and (ii) at the same time improve the quality of its social spending so that it brings
greater benefits for the poor. The Lula administration is committed to this dual policy agenda of
2Figures are from the Instituto de Pesquisa Economic e Aplicada (IPEA), a government research institute.
3 Paes de Barros, 2001.
7



growth-oriented macroeconomic policy and social spending directed at poverty reduction.4 For
both types of policies to be viable, the Government is deepening adjustment to public finances
and carrying out major structural reforms.
3BE.  Growth-Oriented Fiscal Policies
25.   A growth-oriented policy agenda has many facets, and may differ from country to
country. In the context of Brazil, the key policies that are needed to stimulate sustainable and
equitable growth can be subdivided into policies that stimulate investment in physical assets,
5
technological innovation, and upgrading of human capital and skills. Fiscal policies can play a
role in strengthening each of these three growth pillars in Brazil. Growth-oriented fiscal policies
can (i) create a stable macroeconomic environment conducive for investment, (ii) reduce
unnecessary public sector burden, especially the tax burden, on the economy to stimulate
innovation and productivity growth, and (iii) provide needed fiscal space for priority public
investment.
26.   A fundamental prerequisite for sustainable growth is macroeconomic stability. Reducing
the public debt burden and securing debt sustainability is key to achieving stability. Reduced
debt (and thus reduced perceived risks to sustainability) would buttress market confidence and
stimulate investment, which would contribute to higher growth over the long run.
27.   Besides the high level of public debt, the tax system imposes a burden on the economic
growth both because of the high level of taxation and the complicated tax structure. By
emerging market standards, Brazilian tax burden, amounting to 34% of GDP in 2002, is high. In
addition, it features a range of inefficiencies, such as the prevalence of inefficient cascading
taxes that distort relative prices by having differential impacts on firms with different degrees of
vertical integration, high payroll taxes that inhibit employment, and excessive fiscal competition
among states and municipalities which reduces fiscal revenues for sub-national governments and
induces inefficient regional allocation of resources (see Annex on Tax Reform).
28.   All these tendencies worsened over the period of fiscal adjustment starting in 1998,
because of: (i) the increased tax burden during fiscal adjustment due to the expenditure rigidities
that have limited the government's ability to pursue adjustments through expenditure cuts
(explained below); and (ii) the tendency of the federal government to rely increasingly on those
taxes that are not shared with sub-national governments, even though these are precisely the
taxes that have distortionary effects on the economy. Without an overhaul of the current tax
system, Brazil's growth prospects will continue to be compromised, and even the sustainability
of the strong fiscal adjustment effort demonstrated so far could be at jeopardy if society begins to
perceive the continued burdens of the inefficient tax system as intolerable. Therefore, the
Government has launched a tax reform initiative with a view to simplifying tax legislations for
the main state VAT tax (Imposto sobre Comercializa,do de Mercadorias e Servi,os or ICMS),
and reducing the weight of the distortionary taxes in the federal taxes.
4This vision of the administration is clearly expressed in Politica Economica e Reformas Estructurais (Ministerio da
Fazenda, April, 2003).
5 Brazil: The New Growth Agenda, World Bank Report No. 22950-BR, November, 2001.
8



29.   Another manner in which policy can affect long-term growth in Brazil is through public
investment, for example on human capital and infrastructure (e.g., road network to reduce
logistics cost). Because of the fiscal adjustment since 1998, the country had to reduce certain
categories of its public investment sharply in recent years, especially in critical infrastructure.
Even though aggregate public investment has not been shown to enhance growth in Brazil, there
are essential infrastructure needs that continue being unmet in Brazil and are likely to be a
significant growth bottleneck. Reforms that lead to a reduction in unproductive or ineffective
public spending would release funds for essential public investments that could significantly
enhance economic growth.
30.   The most serious constraint to efficient expenditure reallocations and efficient fiscal
adjustment is the problem of budget rigidity. One of the main reasons why Brazil raised
revenues over the period of recent fiscal adjustment, rather than lowering expenditures, is that
the extent of the latter was limited by the system's inherent rigidities. The adjustment would
have been easier and probably less distortionary without the rigidities, because then it would
have been easier to cut low-priority expenditures rather than increase the tax burden on the
economy.
BII.   Distributive Impacts of Fiscal Policies
31.    Aside from growth-oriented policies, poverty reduction depends on distributive impacts
of fiscal policies. Both progressive tax policies and expenditures designed to directly benefit the
poor could contribute to poverty reduction. A recent World Bank study concludes that the
indirect taxes in Brazil are mildly regressive with the poorest 40 percent of the population paying
about 16 percent of total indirect taxes compared to less than 10 percent of their income share.6
Although the direct taxes are progressive, with the poorest 40 percent of the population
contributing only 0.2 percent of the total, these taxes account for less than a third of the total
taxes in Brazil. Thus the overall distributive effects of the Brazilian tax system are dominated by
the regressivity of the indirect taxes, which have increased their share during the last few years
as the government tried to raise additional revenues for fiscal adjustment purposes. Measures to
improve distributive impacts of the indirect taxes are possible, and some are contemplated in the
proposed tax reform. But given the nature of Brazil's tax base (e.g., highly skewed income
distribution), major improvements through changes in tax structures, such as significant increase
in the weight of direct taxes, are unlikely. Thus progressive effects of fiscal policies are
expected from (i) reducing the overall tax burden by shifting to fiscal adjustment through
expenditure cuts; and (ii) improving the effectiveness of pro-poor public expenditures.
32.   The impact of pro-poor expenditures is a function both of their quantity and quality in
terms of the extent to which the spending reaches the poor. As noted above, Brazil's poverty
reduction in recent years has resulted mostly from economic growth, rather than from public
spending directed at the poor. This is because Brazil's social spending has traditionally been
poorly targeted; based on household survey data for 1995-96, a World Bank study estimated that,
on average, only 14% of the resources spend on federal social programs actually reach the
6 Inequality and Economic Development in Brazil, confidential draft, World Bank (June 2002).
9



poorest 20% of the population.7 Much of this spending goes to programs that are known to be
poorly targeted (e.g. the federal social security program, the Unemployment Insurance Program,
higher education and curative medical care in wealthy regions).
33.   There is some evidence, however, of a change in focus regarding the effective use of
social spending for poverty reduction. Over the period of fiscal adjustment that began in
1998/1999, there were two developments that resulted in an increase in the expenditure that
actually reached the poor: (i) total spending on all social programs - including programs that
were poorly targeted - rose from 15.5% of GDP in 1998 to 18% of GDP in 2001; and (ii) there
was a very significant increase in the use of well-targeted programs. Some of the best examples
of the latter include the conditional transfer programs such as Bolsa Escola and the Child Labor
Eradication Program which have effectively reduced short term poverty by directing transfers to
the poorest families and are expected to reduce long-term poverty by breaking the inter-
generational poverty cycle through improved health and education.
34.   The effectiveness of social spending can be made more increased by improving
management, reducing undue duplication of benefits and improving targeting. Further reforms
in these areas could liberate enough resources to fill the gaps that currently still exit in the
Brazilian safety net. The commitment of the Government to evaluate thoroughly the impacts of
existing programs and to improve the national annual household survey so that it becomes a
more useful monitoring and evaluation tool will permit better program design, better
coordination across programs and program expansion on a more effective basis.
35.   There is little evidence that the fiscal adjustment of the last few years adversely affected
the poor. The mildly regressive impacts of the increased indirect taxation have likely been
compensated by the government's conscious efforts to protect social expenditures from spending
cuts and to increase expenditures directed to the poor8 (See Annex on the Impact on Poverty of
Brazilian Fiscal Adjustment for more details). Additional improvements are possible, however,
by reducing the burden of the indirect taxation, creating even more budgetary space for well-
designed and well-managed social programs, and strengthening the targeting mechanisms.
Structural reforms to reduce budget rigidities and institutional reform to strengthen public
expenditure management overall would be crucial for these objectives. In particular, the
proposed social security reforms would begin to reduce one particular inequitable category of
public social spending and thus contribute to the reduction of overall high inequality in Brazil.

III. THE FISCAL REFORM AGENDA: ACHIEVEMENTS AND REMAINING
CHALLENGES
37.    The central objectives of the new administration's economic policy are to regain Brazil's
capacity for sustainable growth, generate employment, and improve income distribution. The
Government recognizes the critical importance of macroeconomic stability, noting the perverse
impacts of inflation and volatility on both sustained growth and on income distribution. The
short-term macroeconomic stability is necessary but insufficient to guarantee resumption of
sustained growth and accelerated social development. The Government is thus committed to
implementing a long-term fiscal adjustment, paying particular attention to its quality to avoid
unnecessary distortions that could result from certain short-term oriented measures. The goals of
this sustained adjustment would be to reduce the debt burden, recover the government's capacity
for maintaining essential public investment and service delivery, and create conditions for
eliminating the pro-cyclical bias in fiscal policy.
38.    Various structural and institutional reforms form part of this agenda, including the major
reforms to the tax and the social security systems that the Government has already launched.
The tax reform is intended to reduce the distortionary impacts of the federal taxes and reduce the
inefficient tax competition among states, and thus facilitate sustainable fiscal adjustment without
jeopardizing growth prospects. A key objective of the social security reform is to reduce the
excessive budget rigidity and create more space for well-targeted social expenditures for poverty
and inequality reduction, in addition to improving the system's long-term actuarial balance and
9 The poverty gap indicator in this diagram was calculated using data from the Pesquisa Mensal de Emprego, a
survey covering only labor income (with social transfers not included) earned in six major metropolitan areas.
Because of this narrow coverage, the data do not accurately capture national poverty trends, but are ideal for
analyzing short-term fluctuations in poverty. Refer to Annex "Impact on Poverty of Brazilian Fiscal Adjustment"
for more details.



correcting the current regressive pattern of benefits distribution. Various measures to improve
the quality of public spending are being considered. For example, institutional strengthening for
social policy targeting, including an adoption of a systematic approach to program impact
evaluations, would contribute to greater efficiency and equity in expenditure allocations and
execution. '0
39.   Based on the Government's stated priorities, the overall fiscal reform strategy can be
characterized as being based on the following four main pillars: (i) institutionalization of
responsible fiscal policy and risk management; (ii) progress toward a more efficient tax system;
(iii) more equitable and efficient public expenditures; and (iv) a more equitable and sustainable
social security system. By the end of the four-year period, Brazil could achieve the following
outcomes through the pursuit of this reform agenda.
o The fiscal system, and hence the economy, would become more resilient to shocks. By
developing further the institutions that reduce uncertainty arising from the fiscal
policymaking process, and by gradually changing the debt composition to reduce rollover,
interest rates, and exchange rate risks, the economy would be less vulnerable to shocks.
o A robust institutional framework for fiscal management mechanisms (either rule-based or
institution-based) would be established to reduce possible market concerns regarding future
fiscal policy and debt sustainability, and facilitate adoption of a fiscal policy that
contemplates the operation of automatic stabilizers subject to the restriction of achieving a
desired medium-term debt level. The resultant boost in market confidence would allow
Brazil to regain full access to market financing, which would then contribute to sustained
growth and poverty reduction.
o The public debt would be stabilized and improved in composition away from the current
structure of high indexation to the dollar and the overnight Selic, and excessive concentration
of short-term maturities. The improved debt composition plus the prudent fiscal policy
envisioned should result in a lower debt ratio, of around 50 percent of GDP in line with the
government target, with a declining trend.
o Both domestic and external debt management would be concentrated in the National
Treasury Secretariat (STN) to ensure greater strategic consistency, and coordinated with the
monetary policy function of the Central Bank.
o Tax reforms would significantly reduce the weight of cumulative taxes in the overall federal
revenue structure, improve the progressivity of the tax structure, curtail inefficient tax
competition among states and thus improve their revenue collection capacities, and further
increase the efficiency of tax administration.
o The social security reform would reduce the burden of the annual deficit of the public sector
system (RPPS) from the current 4% of GDP, and combined with other structural reform
measures, would reduce the degree of budget rigidity to permit more flexible reallocation of
budgetary resources to new government priorities.
10 Based on Politica Econ6mica e Reformas Estruturais, Ministry of Finance (April 2003).
12



* A robust system of program evaluations and public evaluation reviews would be
institutionalized within the annual budget process to improve the government's ability to
improve effectiveness, efficiency and equity of its expenditures through better targeting and
management. This would result in a greatly increased share of total federal social spending
reaching the poorest 20% than the current estimated rate of 14%.
40.   The following section describes in details the main issues and government achievements
in diverse technical areas intended to address the four pillars of the fiscal reform agenda. A
summary of the relevant completed and planned actions is provided in the policy matrix in
Annex 2.
A.    INSTITUTIONALIZATION OF RESPONSIBLE FISCAL POLICY AND RISK MANAGEMENT
41.   This broad category seeks to encompass different actions aimed at creating the
institutions necessary for sustainable fiscal adjustment. In particular it would support actions in
four principal areas: (i) reduction of uncertainty in macro-fiscal management; (ii) further
strengthening of sub-national fiscal discipline; (iii) transparency in fiscal reporting and in the
management of fiscal risks; and (iv) public debt management with respect to internal governance
and development of a secondary market for government securities. The remainder of this section
describes achievements and challenges in each of these areas.
1. Sustainable and credible macro fiscal management
a. Background and Issues
42.   Since the adoption of the Fiscal Stability Program in 1998, the federal government has
sought to reduce the net debt/GDP ratio of the public sector to less than 50% (it was 50% at the
end of 2000). To achieve this outcome, the Government established a target for the primary
surplus of 3.0% in 2001, which would have resulted in a public sector borrowing requirement
(PSBR) of 4.6% of GDP. However, as a result of external shocks during 2001-2002, the
debt/GDP ratio rose to 57% of GDP by the end of 2002 (the ratio reached 63.6% in September).
The unexpected increase in debt was due to the higher-than-expected real interest rate and
greater-than-expected devaluation. In addition, GDP growth was lower than originally expected
in both 2001 and 2002. In response to these shocks, the Government aimed at, and achieved, a
higher primary surplus (3.7%) than originally targeted (3.0%) in 2001 and raised the primary
surplus targets to 3.9% of GDP in 2002 and 3.8% in 2003.
43.   The new Government that started its four-year term in January 2003 soon announced a
primary surplus target of 4.25% for 2003 to signal its own strong commitment to fiscal
discipline. The Government followed up this announcement with a drastic control of budgetary
expenditures in its budgetary and financial programming decree of February 11, 2003. The
February decree revised the estimated net revenue downward from 19.97% of GDP (R$299.5
billion) in the 2003 Annual Budget Law (LOA) to 18.89% (R$301.6 billion).
13



44.     To generate a larger primary surplus target with a reduced revenue (as % of GDP), the
government had to resort to "cutting" expenditures (Table 4).11 Given that close to 80% of the
total authorized in the LOA 2003 were obligatory expenditures, the burden of the expenditure
control fell on discretionary spending, which was reduced from R$62.1 billion (4.1 % of GDP) in
the LOA 2003 to R$48 billion (3.0%) in the decree. The bulk of the "cuts" occurred in non-
social areas, thus continuing the government's conscious effort to preserve budgetary allocations
to the social sectors. Social expenditures were "cut" from R$37.3 billion (2.49%) to R$35
billion (2.19%), whereas the other discretionary expenditures were "cut" from R$24.7 billion
(1.65%) to R$12.9 billion (0.81%).12

45.     In sum, the two successive administrations have continued to demonstrate a strong
commitment to the implementation of a fiscal adjustment program. The initial fear during the
early stages of last year's electoral campaign, that the next administration (irrespective of the
electoral outcome) might not maintain responsible fiscal management, was allayed shortly
thereafter when all major candidates stated their commitment to fiscal responsibility. The latter
demonstrates the degree of national consensus on the importance of fiscal responsibility. The
"These "cuts" are in fact "temporary" withholding of the authorization to incur spending commitments rather than
permanent expenditure cuts. Often, this control is relaxed towards the end of the year and ministries are authorized
to commit more spending than initially authorized in the decree.
12 While these "cuts" in non-social discretionary expenditures appear drastic, it should also be noted that in Brazil,
the approved budget tends to be over-estimated vis-a-vis the realistic revenue capacity. This mostly results from the
addition of parliamentary amendments, that is, inclusion of many expenditure proposals by individual members of
Congress, mostly in the areas of transport and other relatively small-scale capital investment projects. If one takes
this phenomenon into account, the actual extent of cuts from a reasonable level of initial budget allocation would be
much less than it appears from the official figures.
14



decisive measures announced and pursued by the new administration further confirm Brazil's
commitment to responsible fiscal management, an essential basis for fiscal sustainability.
46.    As discussed below, the Law      of Fiscal Responsibility has contributed greatly to
institutionalizing good fiscal management at all levels of the Brazilian federation. At the same
time, the Government is seeking to further improve the credibility of macro fiscal management,
and thus the sustainability of public finance. The Government is committed to maintaining the
primary fiscal surplus required to stabilize the debt to GDP ratio. Thus, a larger primary surplus
might be required under adverse circumstances. In fact, the primary balance plays a dual role as
insurance against future shocks and as a valuable signaling tool to convey to investors the
government's commitment to servicing future debt obligations. Additionally, the Government
recognizes the need to improve the debt composition in favor of longer maturities, which
reinforces the need to reduce debt levels.'3
47.    Debt path simulations show that the public debt-to-GDP ratio would decline in the base
case in which the determinants of the debt ratio (growth, exchange rate, interest rates and
spreads) converge to their most likely values without uncertainty (Table 5), while maintaining a
constant primary surplus of 4.25 percent of GDP. This scenario assumes that the growth rate
converges to 3.8 percent annually (which is the potential growth rate of output according to
Central Bank estimates); that the exchange rate depreciates at a rate equivalent to the difference
between domestic and international inflation, starting from the average forecasted level of 2003
of about R$3.5 per dollar;'4 and that inflation declines slowly to 4 percent, while real interest
rates converge rapidly to 8 percent per year, and sovereign spreads decrease as the debt ratio
falls. This scenario shows that, if the fiscal primary surplus is kept at 4.25 percent of GDP, the
net debt/GDP ratio declines from 57 percent (projected) in 2003 to 50 percent in 2006.15
48.    The above simulations ignore the fact that an economy is subject to random shocks. In
reality, output growth, interest rates and the exchange rate are volatile, and there is some
probability that their true values will differ (perhaps substantially) from their expected values in
either direction. While the last few months demonstrate the possibility of developments that are
more positive than those projected in the base case, the debt dynamics in 2001-2002 are a telling
reminder of the remaining vulnerability of Brazil's fiscal fundamentals.        Although the
government maintained tight fiscal policy for the last several years, its effects on debt
sustainability were counterbalanced by the slow GDP growth, the high real interest rates and
rapid depreciation of the real - circumstances that are largely attributable to unexpected severe
exogenous shocks beyond the government control.

49.    The Annex on Fiscal Sustainability presents several simulations that incorporate
uncertainty, and discusses the implications for the debt sustainability analysis. The main result is
that there is the possibility that the announced 4.25 percent of GDP primary surplus might not be
sufficient to achieve a reduction in public debt ratios. In this sense, an even higher primary
surplus could be viewed as an insurance against future shocks. However, Brazil has a track
record of successfully adjusting further in situations of crisis. On this basis, the current fiscal
stance is considered appropriate.  The Government is now focusing on the quality and
sustainability of adjustment which is the objective of the fiscal reform agenda.

b. Government Actions: Achievements and Challenges
50.   Since 1998, Brazil has faced multiple external shocks arising mostly from international
capital markets. Authorities reacted by increasing the primary balance surplus as needed in
response to the changing external circumstances. This fact differentiated Brazil from other
emerging economies. The LRF and the improvements in the content of the Law of Budget
Guidelines (LDO) were important steps in reducing the uncertainty of future fiscal policy. Since
2000, the Government has introduced explicit medium-term fiscal plans in the LDO Annex on
Fiscal Targets, which establishes, among other things, the target primary surplus and the
expected level for the debt to GDP ratio over a three year horizon. In the draft LDO for 2004
that was submitted to Congress on April 15, the Government set the primary surplus target of
4.25% for 2004-2006. Similarly, the Annex of Fiscal Risks has been revamped since the passage
of the LRF, and now provides a good example of transparent management of contingent
liabilities (discussed below).
51.   The Government is contemplating further improvements to fiscal credibility.  To
maintain a sustainable debt level, such improvements could include the development of an
explicit fiscal framework for setting the primary balance that would allow automatic stabilizers
to operate throughout the business cycle, with set targets for the debt level. The Government has
clearly stated, however, that before adopting such a framework, the medium-term debt ratio
target has to be achieved, making clear that the primary objective is ensuring debt sustainability.
16



2. Sub-national Fiscal Discipline
a. Background and Issues
52.    During the nineties, sub-national fiscal behavior contributed to the deterioration of public
sector accounts. The lack of both efficient sub-national indebtedness controls and federal
government credibility (given the federal government's reputation for refinancing state and
municipal debts) explains some of the irresponsible fiscal behavior of states and municipalities
and the resulting accumulation of sub national debt.
53.   In the course of ten years, the federal government set up three debt restructuring
operations for sub national debt. In 1989, the federal government assumed the states' external
debt amounting to 2 percent of GDP. In 1993, the federal government refinanced the debt that
states and municipalities had with federal financial agencies, totaling an amount equivalent to 7.2
percent of GDP. Finally, in 1997, a program was launched to rescue states' bonded debt, by an
amount equivalent to 11.5 percent of GDP.
b. Government Actions: Achievements and Challenges
54.    With the landmark Law of

Brazilian federation's institutional  municiptos. Note that under Resolution 78, revenues also excluded

regime for ensuring sub-national fiscal discipline has been further buttressed with the issuance of
the two new Senate resolutions (No. 40 and No. 43, December 2001). These Resolutions
maintain that the ratio of personnel expenditure to net current revenue cannot exceed 50% for the
federal government and 60% for states and municipalities, and also establish rules for new
indebtedness by sub-national governments. The ceiling for the ratio of debt to net current
revenue was established at 2 for states and 1.2 for municipalities, and the time frame for the
convergence to these limits was set at 15 years. Limits on credit operations and debt service as
well as strict rules for fiscal management in election years and in the last year of an
administration are other instruments that were implemented to control sub-national indebtedness.
Governments are now prohibited from committing to new expenditures during the last eight
months of their terms if they do not have sufficient cash to pay for such commitments.
17



55.   Brazil has also introduced some measures to control the supply of credit to non-
creditworthy governments. The LRF prohibited credit or debt refinancing operations between
levels of government. Moreover, credit operations of public banks with states and municipalities
that were out of the limits specified in Senate Resolutions 40 and 43 were prohibited.
Resolutions 2827 and 2920 of the National Monetary Council (March and November 2001,
respectively) limit bank exposure to the public sector to 45% of equity - a limitation that is
particularly binding for the Caixa Economica Federal (CEF), which constitutes the most
important source of credit supply for states and municipalities. According to government
figures, net new borrowing by state governments (gross borrowing less amortization) declined
from 22% of net current revenue in 1997 to six percent in 1999. In 2000, amortization exceeded
new borrowing - by three percent of net current revenues. While the stock itself continues to
grow, this is largely due to the capitalization of interest under debt service caps, rather than new
borrowing per se. (See Annex on the Law of Fiscal Responsibility and Sub-national Fiscal
Trends.)

this number increased to 20. In 2001, only five states had their debt to net current revenue ratio
above 2 as established in the Senate Resolution 43.
57.   Positive fiscal results are observed at the municipal level as well. In 2000, despite the
municipal elections, the aggregate balance for Brazilian municipalities was a positive 0.3% with
2,460 local governments (58% of the total) obtaining positive balances. The ratio of personnel
expenditures to net current revenue achieved 0.43 on average with 93% municipalities below the
ceiling of 0.6. At the same time, there was an improvement in the revenue collection efforts by
municipalities, as evidenced in the increased weight of own revenues in net current revenue. In
2001 the municipal fiscal results were even better than in 2000. The aggregate fiscal balance
reached 0.35% of GDP, with 3,375 municipalities (70% of the total) having positive outcomes.
The personnel expenditures to net current revenue ratio stabilized at 43%, with 4,657
municipalities (95.5% of the total) below the LRF ceiling. The consolidated debt of the
18



municipalities was 0.52 of the net current revenue with 4845 municipalities (more than 98% of
the total) below the limit.'16
58.    Thus it is evident that so far the new institutional arrangement introduced with the LRF
and associated legal instruments have positively influenced the fiscal behavior of sub-national
governments. These effects are complemented by efforts to strengthen fiscal management
capacities. To this end, the Government, in partnership with inter alia the Bank, BNDES, and
IDB, has been implementing an extensive fiscal management training program for sub-national
governments, especially small municipal governments.
3. Fiscal Transparency and Planning and Management of Contingent Liabilities
a. Background and Issues
59.    Fiscal transparency is a key element of good macroeconomnic management.           Fiscal
transparency fosters accountability of those who formulate and execute fiscal policies, and can
enhance credibility of fiscal management overall. In recognition of the importance of fiscal
transparency, the LRF introduced a variety of transparency and reporting requirements for all
three level of government in Brazil.
60.    Any analysis of public finance sustainability and transparency must necessarily include
the topic of contingent liabilities and the recognition of past debts that had not been incorporated
previously as public liabilities (or "skeletons" in Brazilian jargon). These types of unexpected
increases in public debt negatively affect the perception of sustainability of public finances, thus
impacting domestic interest and exchange rates, which in turn adversely affect the debt level.
61.    Between 1994-2001 public debt increased substantially as a result of these operations: the
"balance sheet adjustment variations" represent the equivalent of 25 percent of the total change
in net debt during the period (See Table 1 in the Annex on Fiscal Risk and Contingency
Liabilities). Table 7 below provides an explanation of the change in net debt during the 2001-
2002, when total net debt increased 7.2 percentage points, with debt recognitions accounting for
2.3 percentage points of GDP.
16 As a result of these measures, the stock of sub-national bank debt fell by 25% between July of 1999 and July of
2001 (Revenues, in contrast, increased by 31%.) Virtually all the growth in debt has come from increasing
obligations to the Treasury, which reflect (1) new borrowing required to recapitalize state banks prior to their
privatization and (2) the indexation of principal, capitalization of interest, and (in some cases) deferral of
amortization on existing debt owed to the Treasury. Neither source of growth was subject to controls under
Resolution 78 and Lei 9496. External debt also grew, presumably due to the disbursements of multilateral loans that
originated in the Lei 9496 agreements. In relative terms, the ratio of net consolidated debt to net current revenue
was stabilized at about 1.6 level or 16% of GDP in the last three years.
19

b. Government Actions: Achievements and Challenges
62.    With the LRF, the annual LDO has acquired renewed importance as a medium-term (3-
year) fiscal planning tool. Expanding on the LDO's original constitutional role as an instrument
to establish the targets and priorities for the Federal Government, it now must contain the
macroeconomic assumptions underpinning the govemment's fiscal projections and establish
fiscal targets (e.g., the primary balance target), as well as key fiscal aggregates such as total
revenues and expenditures for the three-year period. These practices improve fiscal transparency
by making explicit not only the government's medium-term fiscal strategy but also the basis
upon which such a strategy is built.
63.    A broad set of fiscal data is available with a decreasing period of lag in dissemination at
the National Treasury site. Overall, Brazil's fiscal transparency is of hiph quality, and was
highly rated by the Bank's Country Financial Accountability Assessment 7 as well as by the
IMF's Fiscal Transparency Report on the Observance of Standards and Codes (ROSC).'8
64.    In fact, the frequency and the quality of fiscal reporting has consistently improved over
the last few years. All state governments and a vast majority of municipal governments have
been sending their fiscal accounts to the National Treasury Secretariat (STN) since 2001, and the
STN has published consolidated public sector accounts based on these submissions in 2000
(Portaria #239 of June 2001) and 2001 (Portaria #301 of June of 2002). These consolidations
17 The CFAA also concluded that "notwithstanding the desirable improvements discussed above, the overall
conclusion of this assessment is that the Brazilian system of public financial management provides reliable
information. Adequate systems exist to manage and track the receipt and use of funds at the national level and there
is a high level of fiscal transparency, both of which would support any program of adjustment lending. The risk to
both the Bank and country funds is low." Brazil Country Financial Accountability Assessment (draft for discussion),
June 2002. p. 11.
18 Brazil Country Financial Accountability Assessment, (June 2002). Brazil: Report on Observance of Standards
and Codes (ROSC) - Fiscal Transparency Module, December 2001. IMF Country Report No. 01/217.
20



provide for the first time, an official account of the public sector's overall consolidated revenues
and expenditures. The consolidation comprises the Union, the 27 state governments and 4,500
municipalities (85% of the total number of local governments). Given that the municipal data
includes all the state capitals and all municipalities with more than 50,000 inhabitants, this
consolidation is highly representative. 9
65.    The LRF also requires that the individual government projections from the various LDOs
should be used to generate consolidated government projections, for key fiscal variables. This
would, in turn, enable an analysis of the aggregate fiscal situation of sub-national governments.
In practice, unfortunately, accurate consolidated projections are not yet possible, mainly because
of a lack of uniform standards for reporting the projections. A Fiscal Management Council
(Conselho de Gestdo Fiscal) was to be set up to develop a set of uniform standards for this
purpose, but this has yet to happen. In the absence of a standard reporting framework, the
Government is using a system of standards developed by the Caixa Econ6mica Federal; the
coverage and quality of the consolidated accounts should improve -once the new standards
become available.
66.    With the approval of the LRF, managers are now obligated to report the possible but
uncertain impacts to the budget or public debt resulting from changes in either the
macroeconomic environment or court decisions. Moreover, the LDO contains a Fiscal Risk
Annex that explains multiple sources of risk and lays out budget and public debt sensitivities to
changes in the macro environment. It also lists the main contingent liabilities that public
finances will face in the medium term.20   Finally, a transparent payment schedule has been
established to pay, using government securities (incorporate into public debt), the major
obligations arising from a 2001 decision; labeled the Fundo de Compensa!do de Variaq5es
Salariais (FCVS), this is the biggest of the skeletons.2' (See Annex on Fiscal Risks and
Contingent Liabilities).
67.    Despite improvements to date, there continues to be room for improvement regarding the
budgeting of the risks presented in the LDO Fiscal Risk Annex. Specifically, a desirable action
in the medium term would be to achieve better integration of the fiscal risks described in the
LDO's Fiscal Risk Annex with the Annual Budget Law (LOA). Namely, the contingency fund
could be used as a stabilization fund in which every year some resources could be saved and
used in cases of contingent liabilities materializing into actual obligations.

4. Public Debt Management:
a. Background and Issues
68.    A key issue confronting Brazil's public
debt management is the composition of the debt        Figure 3: Domestic Public Debt
that raises its risk profile. Particular features of  Average Matuntyand Duration of Domestic Public Debt

lengthening debt maturity and deindexation are        1996  1997  1998  1999 2000 2001
