with changes derived from individual empirical distributions for each of the variables, may be overestimating the
true likelihood of all the events happening simultaneously (which implies also overestimating the likelihood of a
growing debt-to-GDP ratio over time). However, the fact that we do not allow for the correlation of shocks over
time may mean (if the shocks are indeed correlated over time) that we are underestimating the likelihood of having a

underestimate the risk that the debt-to-GDP ratio jumps upward (hence also underestimating the pnmary surpluses

towards some historical average over the time (i.e. "mean-reverting property"), then the
appreciating trend hypothesis is very plausible. This is the view adopted by Goldfajn (2002),
who finds that the movement of the real exchange rate towards its 15-year historical average, by
itself, causes the debt-to-GDP ratio to fall by 25 percentage points over a 10-year horizon.
However, statistical tests did not allow us to reject the unit root hypothesis in the real exchange
rate series, implying that the "mean-reverting property" does not exist in the series. This
evidence calls for caution when assuming that an appreciating trend in the debt sustainability
exercises. In assessing the likelihood of future shocks to the real exchange rate in Brazil, it is
important to note the apparent role of capital flows in determining this variable since the 1980s.
During the 1990s, the unpredictable movements in the real exchange rates in other Latin
American countries (i.e. showing appreciating as well as depreciating trends, but with the trends
difficult to predict) highlights the role of external factors in influencing the path of this variable.

15.    For the sake of analysis, we nevertheless considered a scenario that allowed for the full
reversal to mean of the 2003 shocks to the real exchange rate over a 3-year horizon
(acknowledging the fact that some researchers have found mean-reverting characteristics in some
real exchange rate indices for Brazil).53 The scenario is identical to the one described in Table 2,
except that the nominal exchange rate remains fixed at 4.1 Reals per dollar throughout the 2003-
2006 period; the inflation differential between Brazil and the rest of the world, at the same time,
causes the real exchange rate to revert to its mean. In this scenario of full reversal of the
simulated shocks in three.years, an average primary balance of 4.8 percent of GDP would
stabilize the debt-to-GDP ratio at 53 percent.
C. The Signaling Role of the Primary Surplus
16.   In a world of imperfect information, where investors do not know the debt issuer's true
commitment to honoring its obligations, a sovereign entity that is truly committed to pay its
obligations would like to be able to differentiate itself from other types of debt issuers. It could
do so by credibly "signaling" that it is genuine in its intentions to honor its obligations. The
recent contrasting experiences of Argentina and Brazil, as well as several highly indebted
European countries that have faced credibility problems in the recent past, may provide valuable
53 The duration of the shock is estimated by calculating the half-life of the shock in an autoregressive model for the
real exchange rate. See World Bank (2003).
79



lessons in this regard. Despite the similarity in the public indebtedness indicators in Argentina
and Brazil, Argentina suffered a major crisis while Brazil avoided one. The main sustainability
indicator distinguishing Brazil from Argentina is the primary fiscal balance. Can that variable
explain the difference between these countries' fates in the 2000-2001 period?
17.   To examine the primary surplus' role as a signaling device, the model by Drudi and Pratti
(2000) may be useful. It is based on investors' uncertainty regarding the type of government in
office. On the one hand, there are dependable governments that honor their obligations. On the
other hand, there are weak ones that default on their debt obligations. This uncertainty allows a
weak government to issue debt and pretend to be a dependable government. The model predicts
two types of equilibria. One type, a pooling equilibrium, generally prevails at low interest rates
and small debt-to-GDP ratios where, initially, both types of government run primary deficits. In
this equilibrium the dependable government's incentive to distinguish itself from a weak
government is not high, given the low debt-to-GDP ratios or low risk premiums. In the other
type of equilibrium, prevailing when debt-to-GDP ratios are large and the cost of borrowing is
high, dependable governments choose to generate a primary balance surplus to signal their true
nature. Weak governments default because the cost of mimicking the actions of dependable
governments is too high.
18.   According to this model, signaling devices can be used to affect reputation and thus
sustainability; for example, there may be a positive relationship between primary balances and
ratings, and a negative association between debt stocks and ratings. Other models of debt default
with exogenous shocks to interest rates or to government expenditure also predict similar
associations between debt ratings and fiscal variables. For example, a big enough shock to
government expenditure or to interest rates can make debt unsustainable, with a likelihood that
varies positively with the debt ratio and inversely with the primary balance. Herrera (2002)
shows how Brazil's spreads, primary balances and debt ratios show that the primary balance had
a larger impact on spreads as the debt ratio increased, supporting the hypothesis that the primary
balance is a particularly strong signaling device. Drudi and Prati show that the signaling role of
primary balances is also present in four European countries (Italy, Belgium, Denmark and
Ireland) that successfully managed to improve their credit ratings and reduce their public debt
burden.
80

19.   Brazil's management of its primary fiscal balance differentiated it from neighboring
Argentina in the 1999-2001 period. Most debt indicators in Argentina and Brazil were similar
until the end of 2001. And, while, primary balances had been similar for the two countnes until
early 1999, balances afterwards exhibited distinctly different trends (see Figure 2). Argentina's
small primary surpluses contrast with Brazil's significant and growing surpluses. In 1999, the
debt-to-GDP ratio increased in Brazil more than in Argentina; nevertheless, Brazil was able to
maintain a high primary balance as a signal of its commitment to adjustment.
20.    A demonstration of appropriate flexibility in fiscal policy has proved to be an important
signaling tool for investors. Ideally, if debt increases, primary balances need to go up, as a signal
to investors that the government is willing to maintain its commitment to sustainability. The
experience of OECD countries tells us that it is only after a sustained history of primary
surpluses that credibility will be established, and spreads will begin to decline. In Brazil,
sovereign spreads, the public debt-to-GDP ratio and primary balances maintained a stable
equilibrium relationship in the last decade (Herrera, 2002, Fiess, 2002), with spreads being
positively associated with the primary balance and negatively associated with the debt level.
81



This relationship implies that if debt increases, sovereign spreads tend also to rise. Similarly, if
the primary balance rises, spreads tend to fall.
21.   Given this stable relationship between the three variables (primary balance, debt ratios
and sovereign spreads), fixing two of them would determine the value of the third one. For
example, if some fixed target level is chosen for the spreads, and if the debt level is taken as
given, then there would be only one level of primary balances consistent with both. Table 3
illustrates the trade-off that policymakers will face between lowering interest rates (and the cost
of debt) and the magnitude of the fiscal sacrifice that will be required, with the magnitude of the
sacrifice increasing directly with the debt-to-GDP ratio. This present government has given
proof that it is willing to make the sacrifice needed to protect fiscal sustainability.
22.   Since the primary balance estimates in Table 3 are computed as equilibrium values, they
should be interpreted as the structural budget balance needed. That is, the observed figures must
be adjusted for the cyclical components to calculate the structural balance. Brazil had sovereign
spreads below 500 bps in 1993-1994 and in 1996-1997, and the primary balance oscillated
around a mean of 2% of GDP or even lower. However, debt levels were around 32% of GDP.
Under current circumstances, the required primary balances needed to attain similar spread levels
can be attributed to the cost of delayed stabilization, of risky debt composition, and of several
shocks that affected the Brazilian economy, that led to debt accumulation.

ANNEX 5:    THE BRAZILIAN FISCAL RESPONSIBILITY LAW AND SUB-
NATIONAL FISCAL TRENDS
A. Introduction
1.    During the nineties, Brazilian fiscal accounts deteriorated progressively. The end of the
inflationary period in 1994 unmasked the fact that fiscal policy was following an unsustainable
path. Sub national (state and municipal) fiscal behavior contributed to the deterioration of public
sector accounts. There was a lack of efficient control of sub national indebtedness, and the
federal government developed a reputation for frequently bailing out states and municipalities
with financial problems; unsurprisingly, sub national governments exhibited irresponsible fiscal
behavior, and sub national debt rapidly accumulated.
2.    In the course of ten years, the federal government set up three debt restructuring
operations for sub national debt. In 1989, the federal government assumed a portion of states'
external debt which totaled 2 percent of GDP. -In 1993, the federal government refinanced the
debt that states and municipalities had incurred with federal financial agencies, an amount
equivalent to 7.2 percent of GDP. Finally, in 1997, the federal government restructured states'
bonded debt, in an operation amounting to about 11.5 percent of GDP.
3.    Unlike the previous two refinancing operations, the bail-out operation of 1997 was
conditional upon state governments' compliance with fiscal adjustment and structural reform
programs; this was enforced through contracts between the federal government and the states.
Later, the fiscal constraints imposed in these contracts were written into the Fiscal Responsibility
Law (LRF), which also created the institutional framework to regulate fiscal behavior.
4.    The results were impressive and immediate. Sub national fiscal balances have improved
dramatically. State and municipal debt stabilized, despite the adverse macroeconomic
environment characterized by low growth and exchange rate devaluation, factors that ordinarily
have a negative impact on public debt. The aggregate annual primary surplus of states and
municipalities improved by 1.1 percent of GDP, or more than 15 percent of aggregate annual net
current revenue, over the period 1999 to 2002. At the same time, state compliance with the LRF
increased progressively. This report describes the evolution of sub national fiscal discipline and
the achievements of the LRF since its enactment.
B. Overview: Sub national Fiscal Behavior in the Context of the Brazilian Fiscal
Adjustment 1999-2002
5.    Since 1999, the Brazilian government has successfully undergone a period of fiscal
adjustment. In order to pull back from the path of unsustainably large fiscal deficits followed for
most of the nineties, the government embarked on a program of austere fiscal correction in 1999.
As a result, the public sector primary balance rose from a deficit of -0.2 percent of GDP in 1998
to surpluses of 3.25 percent in 1999, 3.5 percent in 2000, 3.7 percent in 2001 and 3.9 percent in
2002.
84



6.    Accompanying the fiscal adjustment effort of the federal government, states and
municipalities have also contributed to the improvement in public sector primary accounts. As
Figure 1 shows, the aggregate primary balance of state and municipal governments has increased
progressively from a deficit of -0.22 percent of GDP in 1999 to a surplus of 0.90 percent of GDP
in 2002; it has been positive for the last 3 years.
Figure 1: Primary Balance of States and Municipalities (in percent of GOP), 1997-2002
0 6

c. 0 2

-06 
-Oa~~~~~~~~~~~~-
7.    This turnaround in sub national fiscal balances can be seen as part of a broader move
towards fiscal contraction, led by the federal government, as a response to the turmoil in
international capital markets since 1998. The positive role that states and municipalities played in
the fiscal adjustment process was facilitated by two factors: (1) the individual state debt
rescheduling agreements with the federal government in 1997-98, and (2) the implementation of
the Fiscal Responsibility Law in 2000. Both of these will be discussed in further detail below.
C. The mechanisms used to induce fiscal responsibility: debt renegotiation contracts (1997)
and the Fiscal Responsibility Law (2000)
8.    In response to the state debt crisis of 1997, the federal government implemented a bail-
out operation, swapping state bonds for federal ones. In exchange for this rescue package, the
federal government negotiated agreements with 25 states in 1997 and 1998.54 Sanctioned by Lei
(Law) 9496 of September 1997, these agreements included: (i) constraints on borrowing, loan
guarantees, debt service and amortization, primary deficits and personnel expenditures; (ii) a
54 Since Tocantins and Amapa did not have any bonded debt, they did not participate in the refinancing agreements.
85



requirement that states privatize their public banks; and (iii) a requirement that states launch
reform programs using the proceeds from the privatization of other assets.
9.    In 1999, Senate Resolution No. 78 institutionalized these agreements and also imposed
fixed ceilings on new borrowing, debt servicing, and the total stock of debt. The most important
constraints imposed were: i) new credit operations could not surpass 18 percent of net current
revenue; ii) interest payments and amortizations could not surpass 13 percent of net current
revenue; iii) the revenue from credit operations could not exceed capital expenditures (the
"golden rule"); iv) the stock of state debt could not exceed 2 times net current revenue in the
period up to 2008, after which the debt stock must be less than or equal to net current revenue; v)
the rollover of the debt could not exceed 95 percent of the debt stock; vi) credit operations based
on anticipated revenues 55 ("Budgetary Revenue Anticipation" or ARO operations) could not
exceed 8 percent of net current revenue; vii) credit operations were forbidden six months before
elections; and viii) credit operations through state enterprises were forbidden.
10.   In addition to these controls on the demand for credit, the federal government also acted
to restrict the supply of credit. First, any further intergovernmental and intra-govemmental debt
refinancing operations were forbidden. In addition, limits were placed on credit operations
between public banks and sub national governments. Resolutions 2827 and 2920 of the National
Monetary Council (passed in March and November of 2001, respectively) limit bank exposure to
public sector borrowers to 45 percent of equity-a limitation that is particularly binding for the
Caixa Econ6mica Federal which constitutes the most important source of credit supply for states
and municipalities.
11.   These controls were strengthened by the Fiscal Responsibility Law (LRF), approved in
May 2000, and by the accompanying Lei de Crimes Fiscais (Law of Fiscal Crimes). The LRF
consolidates many of the restrictions and regulations listed above into one piece of strong
legislation. The three main elements of the LRF are: (i) it explicitly prohibits debt refinancing
operations between different levels of government, eliminating the moral hazard problem that
had existed previously due to the frequent federal government bailouts of the lower-level
governments; (ii) it explicitly mentions that limits are to be placed on personnel costs, credit
operations, loan guarantees and total debt, although it does not state what these specific limits
should be, leaving them to be set later by the Senate in separate resolutions; and (iii) it compels
governments to engage in fiscal planning through the use of annual budget targets.
12.   Senate Resolutions 40 and 43 of December 2001-which are complementary to the LRF
-stated that the ratio of personnel expenditure to net current revenue must be below 50 percent
for the federal government, and below 60 percent for states and municipalities (with specific
ceilings set individually for the Executive, Legislative and Judiciary branches). These Senate
resolutions also imposed a ceiling for the ratio of debt to net current revenue of 2 for states and
1.2 for municipalities; a timeframe of 15 years was set for convergence to these limits. A
comparison between the key constraints of Senate Resolution 78 of 1999 and the subsequent
Resolutions 40 and 43 of 2001, for the states and municipalities, is presented in Table 1; note
55 These are, in effect, funds borrowed by states to address cash-flow problems; they are repayable in principle
within a few months, using fiscal revenues that are anticipated at the time of borrowing to come at a later date. In
practice, these short-term loans often remain unpaid for a much long time than a few months.
86



that the restrictions imposed by Resolutions 40 and 43 are somewhat less stringent than those of
the earlier Resolution 78.

Source: Dillinger (2002)-
13.    The Senate Economic Affairs Commission (CAE) is currently considering putting into
place explicit quantitative limits on the stock of total net federal debt and the amount of gross
outstanding federal securities, as another complementary resolution to the LRF. Because of
macroeconomic volatility in 2002, which adversely affected federal debt, the enactment of these
explicit limits has been delayed. It is currently envisaged that the Senate would establish a
ceiling of 3.5 for the ratio of net federal debt to net current revenue, and 6.5 and for the ratio of
gross federal debt to net current revenue. These limits, when written into a new resolution, would
help complete and consolidate the current LRF.
14.    By 2002, the federal government had already met its obligations under the LRF
Resolutions 40 and 43; expenditure on federal government personnel was 32 percent of net
current revenue in that year. In addition, the federal debt limits under discussion for further LRF
resolutions had also been met; in 2002, net federal debt was 3.14 times net current revenue, and
gross federal debt achieved 5.86 times net current revenue.
15.    Another achievement of the LRF was that it strengthened the role of the Budget
Guidelines Law (Lei de Diretrizes Orcamentarias or LDO) in the budgetary process. The LRF
made it mandatory for governments at all three levels of the Federation to present their LDOs. At
the same time, the LRF established a uniform format and content for states' LDOs; among others
requirements, these had to contain targets for fiscal results over a three-year period (including
targets for both primary and operational balances), thus restricting the possibility of sudden and
unpredictable changes in fiscal policy over that period. The LRF states that the LDO has to
provide projections for key macroeconomic variables and for the ensuing fiscal balances; these
can generate expectations for fiscal outcomes under different assumptions about the economic
87



setting. The LRF also requires that the LDO contain a Fiscal Risk Annex, in order to introduce
transparency and to be able to predict fiscal outcomes in the face of unexpected external shocks.
16.   The LRF also helps regulate longer-term fiscal balances in the following way: It requires
that new and permanent expenditures cannot be created without corresponding increases in
permanent revenue or cuts in other permanent spending items.  During electoral years,
governments are not allowed to make new spending conmmitments unless these can be executed
before the end of the incumbent's term in office. In addition, credit operations are banned in the
8 months prior to each election.
17.   With the help of the LDOs formulated by the different governments in the federation, the
LRF requires that aggregated or consolidated accounts - combining accounts from different
governments - be created. Furthermore, it states that the individual government projections from
the various LDOs should be used to generate consolidated government projections, for key fiscal
variables. This would, in turn, enable an analysis of the aggregate fiscal situation of sub national
governments.
18.   In practice, unfortunately, accurate consolidated projections are not yet possible, mainly
because of a lack of uniform standards for reporting the projections. Currently, the STN is
developing a set of uniform standards for this purpose. The federal government is also providing
training courses to state and municipal administrators on how to formulate their LDOs.
Consolidated public accounts covering most governments in the Federation for the years 2000
and 2001 are available from the National Treasury (STN), using a system of standards developed
by the Caixa Econ6mica Federal; the coverage and quality of the consolidated accounts should
improve once the new standards become available.
19.   The consolidated public accounts available from STN for 2000 and 2001 cover the
federal government, the 27 state governments and 4500 municipalities (which represents 85
percent of the total number of municipalities). As the municipal figures include data from all the
state capitals and all municipalities with more than 50,000 inhabitants, these consolidated data
are highly representative. Three types of information are provided by these consolidated
accounts: (i) the consolidated asset-liability balance, (ii) the consolidated deficit/surplus
calculated through the above-the-line methodology, and (iii) public sector expenditure by
function. 56
20.   The fact that the LRF requires the generation of high-quality consolidated public
accounts illustrates its emphasis on transparency and the dissemination of information, to
enhance credibility and accountability. In this regard, the political commitment of the federal
government and the availability of new information technology were decisive; a broad range of
fiscal data is available, with a short and decreasing period of lag in dissemination, from the
National Treasury site.
21.   The quarterly publication "Boletim  de Financas PNiblicas Estaduais e Municipais"
released by the Central Bank (and available through its website) has been published from May
56 This information is available from the website. www.tesouro.fazenda.gov.br. Individual state and municipal data
are available from the publication "Execu,ao Financeira de Estados e Financas do Brasil- Despesa e Receita dos
Municfpios", available through the same website.
88



1999 to December 2001. In 2002, the government transferred the responsibility of maintaining
this registry from the Central Bank to the STN, which also took over the role of information
dissemination to the public. A GIS-based57 software was also developed to facilitate access by
the public to data on state and municipality debt.
22.   The Federal Government was successful in obtaining public support for the LRF.
Notwithstanding the resistance it initially faced, the LRF probably represents one of the most
successful attempts to build a consensus between public administrators, political parties and
public opinion. The LRF protects governors and mayors from political pressures to raise
spending. Opposition parties are aware of the fiscal performance of current administrations, and
of the need to comply with the limits defined by the LRF. The Federal Government has played
an important role in avoiding past practices such as sub national government debt bailouts, that
undermine the controls on sub national borrowing. Public support for the LRF also was decisive
in increasing the accountability of public administrators. As an example, the run-up to the
municipal elections of 2000 and to the federal and state elections of 2002 have been
characterized by good fiscal performance; in the past, state and municipal primary balances
tended to worsen just before elections were held (Blanco, 2001).
D. The Fiscal Behavior of State Governments
23.   Aggregate data on sub national fiscal accounts reveal the improvement in the fiscal
behavior of states and municipalities during the period of fiscal adjustment. The individual sta.e
analysis presented in Table 2 shows an increasing number of states obtaining positive results
over this period. In 1998, 19 out of 27 states generated primary deficits; in the electoral year of
1998, state primary expenditures were 25 percent higher in real terms than in the previous year,
while tax revenues stagnated due to slow economic growth (of less than 1 percent in 1998). After
the enactment of the LRF, the number of states having primary deficits was reduced to 8 in 2001.
However, some states stand out with high primary deficits that appear to be having trouble in
improving their fiscal balances. These states account for a high share of total state debt, and so
their poor performance in fiscal adjustment constitutes a challenge to the credibility of the LRF.
Below, we offer an explanation for the poor performance of these three states.

states - see Table 3 - show that this ratio went from 1.7 in 2000 to 1.6 in 2001; figures for earlier
years were not available. Thus, at least on average, states are complying with the LRF and
Senate resolutions regarding the size of this ratio, which was limited to 2 (in the 2001
resolutions). The table shows that this ratio showed a falling trend for most states between 2000
and 2001. In 2000, only seven states had a ratio of debt to net current revenue that exceeded the

26.    As Table 4 shows, the limit imposed by the LRF on personnel expenditures is having
positive effects. In aggregate, the ratio of personnel expenditure to net current revenue for the
states improved by almost 10 percentage points over the period 1997 to 2001. In 1997, there
were 16 states where this ratio exceeded the limit of 0.6 established by the LRF; in 2001, there
were only 8 states with a ratio above this limit. Some states that performed particularly badly in
this regard in 2001 were the same states were identified earlier as having high primary fiscal
deficits that were not showing little improving over time. The data in Table 4 indicate that a key
explanation for this is that these states have been having difficulties controlling their personnel
expenditures. Personnel expenditures represent one of the main expenditure rigidities in
government budgets in Brazil; reforms to these structural rigidities are essential to the continuing
success of sub national fiscal reform in the country.

over the last few years; thus, the improvement noted earlier in the ratio of personnel expenditures
to net current revenue resulted not just from revenue increases, but also from a leveling in key

E. The Fiscal Behavior of Municipal Governments, 2000 - 2001
28.    Local governments also contributed to the fiscal improvement of the last three years. In
2000 and 2001, municipalities registered a primary fiscal surplus of about 0.3 percent of GDP in
2000, and about 0.4 percent of GDP in 2001.
29.    Table 6 illustrates the improvement in fiscal indicators between 2000 and 2001;
unfortunately, consolidated data for the municipalities are not available for earlier years, as noted
above. The table shows that, between the two years, there was an increase in the number of
municipalities that sent their fiscal data to STN - who compiled these consolidated data - on
time. In 2001, 78 percent of all municipalities had sent their 2000 fiscal results to STN, within
the time frame established by law. In 2002, this number reached 90 percent. In 2000, in spite of
the municipal elections in that year, the fiscal results were still favorable: the consolidated fiscal
balance for Brazilian municipalities was positive at 0.27 percent of GDP, with 2460 municipal
governments - or 58 percent of the total - registering positive balances. The consolidated ratio of
personnel expenditures to net current revenue was 0.43 in 2000, with 93% of all municipalities
93



below the LRF ceiling of 0.6. This was due mostly to an increase in net current revenue in
previous years, arising from increased tax collection efforts on the part of these governments.
30.   In 2001, the municipal fiscal results were even better than in 2000. The consolidated
fiscal balance reached 0.30 percent of GDP, with 70 percent of all municipalities registering
positive balances. The consolidated ratio of personnel expenditures to net current revenue
stabilized at 0.43, with 95.5% of all municipalities below the LRF ceiling of 0.6. The
consolidated ratio of debt to net current revenues was 0.52, with more than 98% of
municipalities below the established LRF limit of 1.2.
F. Conclusions: Achievements and ChaRlenges Ahead
31.   Compliance with LRF requirements has been impressive. The following can be regarded
as the key fiscal achievements of the last few years: the consolidation of the LRF with the
establishment of ceilings on debt and on personnel expenditures relative to net current revenues;
the increasing compliance with these ceilings; the public support for the LRF; and of course the
turnaround in sub national fiscal balances.
32.   However, the main threat to continued compliance with the LRF is the high rigidity in
sub national public expenditures, which limit the scope of cuts in current expenditures. Personnel
expenditures and especially social security benefits are the main sources of this rigidity.
Structural reforms in these areas are vital for the continuation of good sub national fiscal
performance.
33.   A number of steps remain to be taken regarding the LRF itself and the associated
budgetary process. First, the effort to consolidate the state and municipal accounts, and their
LDO projections, needs to continue. And finally, a Congressional resolution for the imposition of
ceilings on federal government debt-as required by the LRF-has yet to be approved.

ANNEX 6: FISCAL RISKS AND CONTINGENT LIABILITIES IN BRAZIL
A. Introduction
1.         Contingent liabilities account for a significant portion of net debt growth between
1994 and 2001 (see Table 1, line "Balance Sheet Adjustment"). Unexpected increases in the
level of debt adversely affect perceptions of public debt sustainability, which in turn affect
interest rates and the exchange rate. This gives rise to a second round of adverse impacts on
the debt level, and a vicious cycle ensues of debt increments, more interest rates hikes, more
debt increases, and so on. This is one major reason why the Law of Fiscal Responsibility
(LRF)-approved                 in 2000-is crucial; it strongly emphasizes increased transparency
regarding possible unexpected future changes in the debt stock.

2.         The Annex on Fiscal Risks is published as part of the Budget Guidelines Law (Lei de
Diretrizes OrVamentdrias) or LDO, which governments at all three levels of the Federation
are obliged to produce annually under the LRF. This Annex contains a comprehensive
coverage of fiscal risks and contingent liabilities. Information on past liabilities arising from
5S See "Public Debt Management, Monetary Policy and Financial Institutions" by Garcia M., in 2000.
Background Paper for World Bank Country Economic Memorandum, Brazil. Washington, D.C
95



court decisions must be included in the annex; these liabilities must eventually be
incorporated into public debt. The Annex made by each government is available from the
Ministerio do Planejamento website www.planejamento.gov.br. The remainder of this annex
is a textual reproduction of material found in the Planning Ministry website, which allows the
reader to judge the quality and transparency of the discussion.
B. Fiscal Risks
3.    The LRF gives prominence to the identification of fiscal risks in the annexes of the
LDO. This analysis provides an indication of: (i) the medium and long-term prospects of the
fiscal situation of the federal governments, and: (ii) the magnitude of possible adjustments
that the government may make in the short-term, in response to unforeseen changes in the
macroeconomic environment or to unanticipated court decisions. The transparency regarding
risks as well as about contingent liabilities haye been a priority of the present administration,
which undertook the task of cleaning up fiscal accounts. The policy of broad disclosure of the
so-called "skeletons" has resulted in the identification of hidden liabilities equivalent to more
than 10% of GDP in recent years.
4.    The identification of several risks, including that from  lawsuits, was already
accomplished in the 2002 LDO. As had happened previously, some of the risks identified at
the time ended up being resolved in ways favorable to the government. This is the case, for
instance, with the long-lasting dispute about the tax immunity of pension funds.  A
combination of a few favorable court decisions and a special program designed by the
government resulted in the dropping of many court actions and the payment to the
government of about R$7 billion in income taxes.
5.    Similarly, the dispute over the adjustment of balances in the severance payment fund
for the private sector (FGTS)-related to economic plans in 1989 and 1990-was solved
through an agreement that has been ratified by Congress. This outcome has shielded the
government from an indirect risk once estimated at close to R$40 billion.
6.    From a conceptual standpoint, fiscal risks can be divided into two main categories:
budgetary risks and debt risks. Budgetary risks are related to deviations of revenues or
expenditures from their projected values. Debt risks are mainly related to the impact of
interest and exchange rates on the public debt, as well as the potential impacts of contingent
assets and liabilities.
Budgetary Risks:
7.    Among the main budgetary risks are those linked to unexpected changes in GDP
growth. A 50 basis points increase in GDP growth (reduction), for instance, results in a 50
basis points increase (decrease) in gross primary revenues of the central government, or
roughly R$1.6 billion.
8.    The exchange rate can also have a significant impact on the revenues from the import
tax, the Industrial Products Tax and the Income Tax on Capital Remittance. These taxes
96



account for 8.8% of the total administered revenues in 2003. For each 1% change in the
exchange rate (holding the tax base constant in US$ dollar terms), there is a change of about
R$200 million in revenues over a 12-month period.
9.    The level of   economic activity also affects some categories of expenditures.
Unemployment tends to rise when the economy slows down, while personnel expenditures
and social benefits are mainly determined by decisions related to career plans and raises in
wages. A R$1.00 increase in the minimum wage, for instance, results in a R$183.0 million
net growth of social security and social assistance expenditures in a 12-month period, while a
1% raise in federal government personnel expenditures results in R$730 million additional
outlays per year.
10.   To deal with any unanticipated changes in spending or revenue during the year, the
Fiscal Responsibility Law (Article 9) requires a bimonthly reassessment of revenues, to match
the budgetary and financial execution to the fiscal targets established by the LDO. This
bimonthly reassessment, together with the compliance review of intra annual fiscal targets
(done on a four-month basis), is one of the most powerful tools of the LRF, as it permits the
quick correction of fiscal slippages.
Debt Risks:
11.   The exchange rate is also associated with debt risks. The exchange rate is also
associated with debt risks. Given the current structure of the public debt, a 1% devaluation of
the Real increases the debt to GDP ratio by 23 basis points, while a 100 basis point increase in
the interest rate (SELIC) increases the debt to GDP ratio by 24 basis points over a 12-month
period. (See Table 2.)
12.   Another source of debt risks are contingent liabilities, which are by definition
unpredictable in their ultimate size and impact. These include court claims against the Federal
Government and the so-called "skeletons" or old debts that are now being recognized and
incorporated into the Treasury debt. The contingent liabilities can be classified by origin into
at least six classes. A large portion of the claims relates to issues surrounding the effects of
price controls or the monetary correction of liabilities that happened before the Real Plan, i.e.,
at the time of high inflation. Of course, the measurement of these potential liabilities is
difficult and often imprecise.
13.   It is important to note that the listing in the LDO Annexes of the potential liabilities
due to pending court decisions does not affect the outcome of thee cases; the Federal
government is committed to defend the legality of its actions. Rather, the listing is aimed at
reinforcing fiscal transparency and providing to society a better understanding of potential
fiscal pressures.
97

14.   Some of the liabilities listed in the Annex of Fiscal Risks are denominated in pre-1994
local currency; thus, their real value depends on the indexation system used. Examples
include the expropriations (in "Cruzados") of the Collor Plan, and the several court actions
from the sugar, alcohol and aviation industries related to price controls. The dispute over
adjustment of balances in the severance payment fund for the private sector (FGTS) centers
on liabilities of a fund that is essentially; nevertheless, the National Treasury has partial
responsibility for the solvency of this fund, and it is important to see Supplementary Law Nr.
110-passed in 2000 in an attempt to resolve this issue--will be implemented. Under this
law, two social contributions were introduced to finance the payments to be made-an
estimated R$40 billion in total. All participants will be entitled to an adjustment that is
proportional in size to the amount that they had originally invested in the fund.
15.   Other fiscal risks are associated with the closure of public enterprises, organs as well
as other administrative acts. The Federal Railroad Company (Rede Ferroviaria Federal S/A -
RFFSA) closed in 1999, but the sales of its assets has proved difficult, because of the size of
the company (75,000 non-operational assets) and because the company is burdened by more
than 37,000 court actions, from 120,000 forner employees. The potential cost of the court
cases is R$4.5 billion. Another fiscal risk arises from the R$11.4 billion set aside to cover
payments to individuals disputing tax decisions in court; so far, approximately R$1.1 of this
has been returned to tax payers.
16.   The Central Bank also has some contingent liabilities. One of the banks under
liquidation, for instance, claims that part of its losses can be ascribed to some official
agricultural credits dating back to 1988-90, and it is seeking restitution. The Bank also faces
risks associated with its R$27 billion exposure to balance sheet items inherited from other
98



financial institutions under liquidation. The housing system is the source of the most so-called
"skeletons"-about R$60 billion in total, of which a large part has already been incorporated
into the total debt.

17.   Fiscal risks are not all negative; there are some contingent assets too. In particular, the
claims of the Treasury against others exceed R$150 billion, of which R$6 billion might be
paid in 2002 (R$ 5.3 billion were paid in 2001). The Social Security (INSS) has credits of
more than R$75 billion, of which about 25% are now being paid through installments allowed
by the REFIS (Programa de Recupera.do Fiscal) back taxes payment program.
C. Social Security Actuarial Evaluation
18.   The LRF requires the actuarial evaluation of all public pension schemes, as well the
provision of future projections, to facilitate the required risk analysis regarding the budget as a
whole. This has been carried out with increasing detail under standard assumptions used in the
profession.
CI. Executive and Judiciary Civil Servants
19.   The civil servants social security regime is a pay-as-you-go system funded by
contributions from employees and transfers from the federal budget. The actuarial projections
of civil servants' social security in the LDO indicate an increase of the annual deficit from
1.03% of the GDP in 2001, to 1.11% of the GDP in 2002. From then on there is a decreasing
trend, with the figure reaching 0.11 % of the GDP in 2076, mainly due to a projected reduction
of public servants in the wake of reform of the State.
CII. General Social Security Regime (RGPS)
20.   The RGPS is the basic social security regime for the private sector. This is a pay-as-
you-go scheme financed by employees and employers. Since 1995, with the rapid growth
of-among others-pensions to rural workers, contributions to the RGPS have not been
enough to fund its benefits, with deficits being covered by other social contributions.
99



21.   The LDO provides an analysis of the long-term prospects of the RGPS; this indicates
that the deficit of the system, after initially falling in the coming few years, will move back up
to about 1.2% of GDP and remain there in the next decades. This stabilization is related to the
social security reform measures implemented since December, 1998, in the wake of the
Constitutional Amendment Nr. 20, of 1998, as well as laws which have succeeded in
improving mechanisms of tax collection.
CIII. Financial Analysis of the Social Security Organic Law (LOAS)
22.   The analysis presents long-term projections for the social assistance benefits to the
aged and the handicapped, as established by the LOAS (Law nr. 8,742 of December 07 de
1993). These social assistance benefits are paid to people who are older than 67 or
handicapped, and who come from families with an average income below 1/4 of the minimum
wage. The benefit provided is one minimum wage.
23.   The number of beneficiaries has shown a remarkable increase in recent years. This
number will continue to grow, from 1.4 million in 2002 to more than 2.8 million in 2021.
Assuming that the minimum wage grows with inflation, projected LOAS outlays will more
than double in the next 15-20 years, according to the LDO, eventually accounting for 0.25-
0.28% of the GDP.
CIV. Financial Standing of the FAT
24.   The Fundo de Amparo aos Trabalhadores (FAT) is a fund subordinated to the
Ministry of Labor. It finances the unemployment insurance scheme and the bonus paid to
earners of wages close to the minimum wage (abono salarial). In addition, as required by the
Constitution, a portion of FAT resources is deposited at the development bank Banco
Nacional de Desenvolvimento Economic e Social (BNDES), to be lent to firms. This fund is
projected to show a surplus balance until 2005.
100



ANNEX 7: PUBLIC DEBT MANAGEMENT: GOVERNANCE, CAPACITY, AND
MARKET DEVELOPMENT
A. Introduction
1.    The Brazilian Ministry of.Finance has made substantial progress in recent years
towards establishing a sounder institutional, procedural, and technical arrangements to
manage public debt. This process-which benefited considerably from World Bank TA loan
resources and expert staff-has proceeded more or less in parallel with the strengthening of
fiscal policy and its recent institutionalization under the Fiscal Responsibility Law (approved
in May 2000).
2.    However, repeated episodes of external shocks and financial turbulence since the 1998
Russia crisis, especially hard-hitting in late-1998 to early-1999 and during 2002, created a
such a high level of systemic risk that the authorities could not ignore when pursuing debt
management and monetary policy objectives. The constraints posed by investor jitters and
macroeconomic turbulence were complicated by the limits posed by market structures and
capacity to absorb risk on the authorities' ability to implement their desired long-term debt
management strategy. Against this background, the authorities at the Ministry of Finance had
to shift towards the issuance of SELIC-indexed debt securities in order to achieve debt
rollover in a hostile environment.  (They managed, however, to avoid loosing too much
ground on the gains made in reducing rollover risk relative to pre-1998 levels.) Similarly, the
authorities at the Central Bank had to give a high priority to mitigating asset price
(particularly exchange rate) volatility, which induced them not just to sell dollars spot but also
to provide hedges against exchange-risk to the private sector. The latter took mainly the form
of placements of Central Bank-issued dollar-linked debt initially (mainly in late-1998 and
1999), and Central Bank-issued currency swaps subsequently (mainly in 2002). Turbulence-
contingent debt strategies did facilitate debt rollover when turbulence surged, and the issuance
of dollar-liked debt and currency swaps by the Central Bank did dampen exchange rate
volatility, but at the cost of a massive transfer of market (interest and exchange rate) risk to
the balance sheets of the government and the Central Bank.
3.    The legacy of such developments is that of a complicated composition of federal
liabilities-i.e., debt that unduly exposes its issuer to market risk-which might weaken the
resiliency of the fiscal process. This legacy, combined with limits to the current ability of
markets to absorb risk, concentrated market structures, and certain shortcomings in the fund
industry, constitute a challenging initial condition-one that significantly constraints the
speed at which steady progress can be made in reducing debt exposure to market risk.
4.    Notwithstanding the episodes of financial turbulence, visible progress did take place
with regard to the framework and organizational arrangements for debt management,
especially since 2000, as detailed below. And all along the government has held fast to, and
even over-performed, its fiscal objectives, with the 2003 target for the fiscal primary surplus
having been raised by the current administration to 4.25 percent of GDP. The clear progress
101



in these fronts over the past years, combined with the recent return of tranquil times in the
macroeconomic and financial spheres, opens a unique opportunity for the government to
make significant advances in public debt management and in developing more efficient
markets for public debt. If this opportunity is adequately seized by the government, a gradual,
sustainable reduction in the exposure of public debt to risk can be realistically envisioned.
5.    In what follows, this annex first examines the recent evolution in the composition of
the federal domestic debt, and extracts some implications and lesson. Subsequently, the
annex discusses the government's efforts made to date in improving public debt management
and in fostering the development the markets for such debt, as well as the government's plans
to intensify such efforts in the future.
B. Composition of the lfDomestic Federal Debt
1.    Recent Evolution
6.    Despite the shift from traditional deficits to continued and rising fiscal primary
surpluses since 1999, Brazil has been unable to significantly reduce the exposure of its
domestic federal debt to market (i.e., interest and exchange rate) risk, although it has been
able to broadly retain the gains made in reducing rollover risk after 1998. A key reason for
this outcome lies in the repeated episodes of macroeconomic financial turbulence (triggered
by external shocks) that have affected Brazil since the second half of 1998, particularly in
late-1998 to early-1999 and during 2002. Another reason related to market constraints-
particularly the limits to market capacity to absorb and manage large amounts of risk. Given
such macroeconomic turbulence and micro/market constraints, the Treasury felt compelled to
issue SELIC-indexed debt to facilitate debt rollover in the midst of a hostile environment.
And the Central Bank felt compelled to issue dollar-linked debt (mainly in late-1998 and
1999) and currency swaps (in 2002), in order to dampen asset price volatility. The legacy has
been a sovereign domestic debt that is highly exposed to market risk, more today than it was
in mid-1998, although less exposed to rollover risk.
7.    In effect, as of May 1998, more than half of the federal domestic debt held outside the
Central Bank was denominated in Reais and had a fixed interest rate, while dollar-linked and
SELIC-indexed debt constituted 28 and 17 percent of the total, respectively (Appendix Figure
1). In contrast, by March 2003 the proportion of fixed interest rate debt in the total had
collapsed to only 2 percent, while the shares of dollar-linked debt (including swaps issued by
the Central Bank) and SELIC-indexed debt (net of swaps) had risen to 35 and 50 percent,
respectively.
8.    Two phases can be identified in the evolution of debt composition between the two
points in time mentioned above. The first phase was unleashed by the contagion from the
Russia crisis, which led to the floating of the Real in early-1999, and lasted roughly until late-
2001 (when Brazil was hit by the aftermath of terrorist attack of September 11, the bursting of
the U.S. stock market bubble, and the Argentine crisis).
102



9.     The Russia crisis found Brazil with a relatively moderate exposure of Government
debt to interest and exchange rate risk, as noted in the previous paragraph, but a relatively
high exposure to rollover risk, with more than one-half of the stock of domestic debt
scheduled to fall due in the subsequent 12 months. In response, the authorities made it a
priority to reduce rollover risk by extending debt maturity, even if in doing so they had to
increase the shares of SELIC-indexed debt, thereby accepting a rise in debt exposure to
interest rate risk. At the same time, in response to confront the pressures in the exchange
market, the Central Bank issued large amounts of dollar-linked debt.
10.    The authorities were successful in their endeavor to reduce rollover risk. The average
maturity of (auctioned) debt, which had been steadily increasing since 1995, rose steeply from
about 8 months in 1998 to about 30 months by September 2001, before falling to 22 months
by March 2003, as a result of the times of distress in 2002. Similarly, the proportion of debt
falling due in the- next 12 months decreased from 53 percent at end-1999 to 26 percent at end-
2001 (Appendix Table 1). In the process, however, there was a nearly instantaneous, massive
switch from fixed interest rate debt to SELIC-indexed debt early in the period-the so-called
"scissors" effect in the Fall of 1998 and the share of dollar linked debt held outside the
Central Bank rose steadily (Appendix Figure 2).
11.    The second phase unfolded during 2002. Macroeconomic and financial turbulence
was such in that year (e.g., country risk rose to 2000 basis points by mid-year and the Real hit
a high of nearly 4 Reais per dollar in October) that the authorities felt compelled to accept a
major increase in debt exposure to risk in order to (1) facilitate debt rollover in the midst of a
hostile environment; and (ii) dampen the already high volatility in asset prices.
12.    The latter objective was pursued by the Central Bank through monetary operations
that had a feedback effect on debt composition. In particular, the Central Bank used its large
holdings of government debt to conduct debt exchanges, which shortened the maturity of
public debt held by the market, raising the share of fixed interest rate debt in the portfolio of
government debt paper held by the Central Bank.59 It also engaged in cash debt buybacks,
reducing debt in exchange for an expansion in money base. Finally, it placed (starting in May
2002) a large amount of swap contracts to enable market participant to hedge their exposure
to exchange rate risk (by swapping Reais interest income with dollar-linked interest income,
60
with daily settlement).
13.    As a result, the exposure of the government's debt portfolio (including Central Bank-
issued swap contracts) to risk, particularly market risk, increased significantly. Rollover risk
exposure increased somewhat, as measured by the fraction of debt falling due in the next 12
months, which rose from 26 percent at end-2001 to 39 percent at end-2002 (Appendix Table
1), although it remained below the high pre-1999 levels. Exchange rate risk exposure
59 As of March 2003, the amount of fixed interest rate debt held by the Central Bank was about 3 times of that
held by the market The Central Bank's holdings of dollar-linked and SELIC-indexed debt were equivalent to,
respectively, about 70 and 40 percent of the stocks held by the market (Appendix Figure 3).
60 In the swap contracts, the dollar-linked flow that the Central Bank agrees to pay is calculated in dollars but
settled in Reais, daily, at the exchange rate of the settlement date. The government's dollar-linked debt
contracts are also are settled in Reais.
103



augmented as measured by the share of dollar-linked debt (including the notional amounts of
Central Bank-issued currency swaps), which grew from 29 to 37 percent of the same period
(Appendix Figure 2). By contrast, exposure to interest rate risk shrank as measured by the
6!
share of SELIC-indexed debt (net of swaps), which declined from about 57 to 48 percent.
At the same time, the stock of currency swaps issued by the Central Bank surged to the
equivalent of US$31 billion as of March 2003 (compared to a stock of international reserves
of about US$38 billion, as defined by the Central Bank, and about US$14 billion, using the
IMF definition of liquid international reserves).
2.    Implications and Lessons
14.   There is little doubt that the structure of federal debt is a reflection of the volatile
environment in which Brazil has lived over the past 3 years or so. At the same time, such
structure raises the vulnerability of public finances and the economy in general to shocks and
to self-fulfilling attacks. The composition of federal debt can weaken the resilience of the
fiscal process-which Brazil has made commendable strides to strengthen-through at least
two channels, as illustrated by the experience of last year.
15.   First, there is a potential for a perverse feed back loop between debt composition and
concerns about debt viability. To facilitate debt rollover and mitigate volatility, the
government increases the share of shorter-duration (SELIC-indexed) debt. This can worsen
the fragility of the debt profile which, in turn, can intensify investor concerns, and so on.
16.   Second, the high exposure of domestic debt to market risk could undermine the
credibility of the inflation targeting framework. For instance, a major depreciation of the Real
induced by an external shock would, on the one hand, complicate debt dynamics because of
its impact on dollar-linked debt and currency swap exposure; on the other hand, it could raise
inflationary expectations. If the Central Bank responds by increasing the interest rate, that
would also complicate debt dynamics because of its direct impact on SELIC-indexed debt.
Complications in debt dynamics and particularly in the rollover process, could also put
pressure on the Central Bank to engage in debt buybacks, that is, in retiring debt in exchange
for an expansion of the money base which, in turn, could undermine the achievement of the
inflation target.
17.   Given the evolution of debt composition and considerations above, the authorities are
committed to raising the priority assigned to the medium-term objective of reducing the.
exposure of public debt to rollover, interest, and exchange rate risks. Such a renewed
emphasis is enhanced by the current convergence of two favorable conditions. Firstly, as
described in more detail in the next section, considerable progress has already taken place in
the implementation of the government's agenda towards establishing sounder institutional and
governance arrangements, as well as building technical capacity, for the management of debt.
Also, important actions have been taken to foster more efficient markets for government debt.
61 To simplify Appendix Figure 2, SELIC-indexed debt is defined to include the small share of floating interest
rate debt known as reference rate debt. At end-2002, such share was only 2 percent of the total.
104



18.   Secondly, the macroeconomic and financial context has substantially stabilized.
Country risk has fallen drastically, to below 800 basis points above U.S. Treasuries, and the
Real has appreciated significantly, reflecting a frank resumption of investor confidence in
response to the authorities' commitment to sound economic policies.
19.   The convergence of these two favorable conditions significantly boost the prospects of
success, and hence militates in favor of intensifying the implementation of the reform agenda
in the areas of public debt management and debt market development. In addition, the fresh
lessons learned during the turbulent last year have opened a window of opportunity for reform
in specific areas-such as the regulation of funds.
20.   However, the complexity of the initial conditions poses a major constraints on the
feasible speed at which progress can be achieved towards reducing the exposure of public
debt to risk. This is likely to be a slow, gradual process. Initial conditions are complex not
just because public debt is highly exposed to market risk, the unwinding of which is
inherently difficult. They are complex also because of concentrated market structures and
certain shortcomings in the fund industry (see Section D below), which limit the speed at
which risks can be prudently transferred from the government balance. Debt composition and
market features conspire to limit the speed at which the government can unwind the existing
moral hazard (i.e., market participants have come to expect that the government or the central
bank will provide risk-protection in times of turbulence) in ways that do not destabilize the
exchange and interest rates.
C. Debt Management: Governance and Capacity
1.    Achievements to Date
21.   There have been visible achievements in various dimensions of debt management in
recent years, aided by technical assistance from World Bank experts and the resources form a
World Bank TA loan. The process of transferring domestic debt issuance responsibilities
from the Central Bank to the National Treasury Department (STN) within the Ministry of
Finance has been completed. The Central Bank ceased issuing its own debt. The government
is moving towards transferring the responsibility for external debt issuance from the Central
Bank to STN.
22.   Concomitantly, the STN has introduced important organizational changes, including
the creation of the Debt Management Office (DMO). Also, an informal Debt Management
Committee (DMC) has been set up to discuss strategy and funding decisions. And the STN
has been making steady progress in implementing improved operational risk safeguards, in
line with the recommendations of recent reports by specialized consultants.
23.   At the same time, the technical capacity of the DMO, particularly the Middle and
Front Offices, has been strengthened considerably. The DMO has attracted highly qualified
staff using incentive systems such as financing of post-graduate studies and ample training
opportunities, as well as by the challenges presented to them. The Middle Office has built the
capacity to carry out forward looking simulation analysis and to develop a strategy based on
105



cost and risk trade-offs. Its staff has a deep understanding of various issues involved in the
analytical process and have produced high-quality published and unpublished reports to
support decision makers and to inform the public.
24.   Supported by these improvements in organizational arrangements and technical
capacity, the Ministry of Finance started issuing highly informative and technically sound
Annual Borrowing Plans in 2001. These Plans have increasingly highlighted the importance
or risk (and not just cost) in the debt management strategy, in a manner consistent with the
main objective is to minimize cost over the long term, taking into consideration the
maintenance of judicious levels of risk.
2.    Salient Elements of the Pending Reform Agenda
25.   As noted earlier, the authorities intend to seize the opportunities afforded by the
current favorable juncture to intensify the implementation of its reform agenda for debt
management, building on the major progress made to date, in order to enhance the resiliency
of the overall fiscal process to shocks. To this end, the debt management framework will
make further progress in explicitly incorporating risk (and not just cost) considerations, while
further actions will be taken to ensure that the debt management process is endowed with
improved governance arrangements and high standards of transparency and accountability.
The government sees this effort as an essential complement to the LRF in securing public debt
viability, itself a necessary condition for a permanent reduction in country risk and a sustained
path of higher output and employment growth.
26.   The government plans to significantly advance in the process of transferring the
authority for the issuance of the government's external debt from the Central bank to STN.
The Front Office capacity in the DMO will be strengthened to adequately assume these new
responsibilities.  In addition, the DMO  will continue implementing its institutional
strengthening program, through the streamlining of internal processes, the enhancement of
operational risk controls (including the creation of a Compliance Unit in the STN and the
Internal Auditor for the DMO), improving data management in the Back Office, and
enhancing the IT systems.
27.   In the governance front, the Ministry of Finance will soon formalize the Debt
Management Committee within STN. In addition, the government plans to create a Public
Debt Council (PDC) to be headed by the Minister of Finance. The PDC will, among other
things, approve the government debt management strategy and the associated Annual
Borrowing Plans.  These new  governance arrangements-and the transparency and
accountability they entail-should significantly enhance discipline in the  process of
formulation, approval, monitoring, and evaluation of the government debt management
strategy.
28.   As regards the formulation of the debt management strategy, the National Treasury
will assess the pros and cons of migrating towards a single strategy for the Annual Borrowing
Plan, with appropriate margins to accommodate volatility and provisions for revision of the
Plan when there is a fundamental change in the structural relationships between variables. (At
106



present, given uncertainty and volatility, the Annual Borrowing Plan contemplates 4 different
combinations of macro and market scenarios that set wide ranges for the debt risk indicators
to be achieved under the Plan.) To this end, the DMO plans to design a funding strategy
based on a cost/risk analysis and initiate a trial period by analyzing it in the DMC, in parallel
to the current approach. As macroeconomic volatility narrow and market development
progresses, the government intends to assess the merits of possibly moving towards a
portfolio benchmark approach, in order to gradually narrow the band-width of debt risk
indicators to be achieved under the Borrowing Plans.
29.   The specific guidelines for debt management in 2003, as stated in the Annual
Borrowing Plan, are summarized in table below. The target ranges for debt issuance are wide,
given that the guidelines are set to accommodate very different scenarios. In practice,
however, the DMO follows only one funding strategy-the one consistent with the scenario
that more closely resembles actual developments. As the funding strategy is designed to be
state-contingent, it would be modified should a radical change in macroeconomic conditions
occur.
Guidelines for Federal Domestic Debt Issuance in 2003
(Percentage Composition of Debt Stock Held Outside the Central Bank)

*  Nominal fixed interest rate    5          15        2.2
