Source. Ministry of Finance, Annual Borrowing Plan.
30.   While the Central Bank has ceased to issue its own debt paper, in line with provisions
under the LRF, it can still alter the risk composition of government debt held by the market,
As noted in Section B. It can do so through (i) the auctioning of currency-risk hedges
(swaps), as part of its operations to mitigate exchange rate volatility; and (ii) debt exchanges
(using its own large inventory of government debt paper) and debt buybacks, as part of its
operations to mitigate interest rate volatility. Given the complexity of the initial conditions
(troublesome composition of government debt outstanding, high stock of currency swap
contracts, remaining potential for macroeconomic volatility, and certain market features), the
Central Bank needs to retain the full array of monetary and exchange rate operations, even if
their use leads to deviations from the objectives regarding government debt composition in
the Annual Financial Plans, forcing a revision of the targets under such Plans.
31.   Against this background, the government plans to strengthen the coordination between
the Ministry of Finance and the Central Bank. Moreover, Central Bank policy is guided by
the medium-run objective of unwinding in an orderly fashion its currency swap book, as
macroeconomic and market conditions permit. In the shorter-term, and also as circumstances
107



permit, the Central Bank stands ready to rollover the notional principal of currency swaps
falling due, while endeavoring to avoid increasing its exposure therein.
D. Debt Management: Market lfDevelopment
32.   Efforts to strengthen the governance and strategy for debt management will need to be
complemented by a structured and well-sequenced approach to the development of the local
market for government debt. The actions to be taken by the authorities can be grouped into
three areas: (i) further refinement and development of the approach to the issuance of
government securities by STN; (ii) improvement in the size and diversity of the end investor
base; and (iii) creation of greater liquidity in the secondary market for government debt.
Actions in these three areas are mutually reinforcing and their implementation would enhance
the efficiency of primary and secondary markets for government debt and, hence, broaden the
scope for success in the government endeavors to reduce -the exposure of its debt to risk.
Actions already taken and planned in each area are described below.
1.    Reorienting the Domestic Debt Issuance Strategy
33.   The authorities have over the last couple of years revamped their strategy for the
issuance of domestic debt in significant ways. The transparency and predictability of issuance
of government securities has improved through the publication of issuance calendars, and the
mentioned consolidation of domestic debt issuance in STN. The authorities at the Ministry of
Finance have, furthermore, reduced the frequency of debt auctions and concentrated issuance
of new debt in a fewer number of securities, while applying buyback operations to further
reduce the fragmentation of outstanding issues. These initiatives are important to deepen and
increase efficiency of primary and secondary markets for government securities.
34.   As outlined in the Annual Borrowing Plan and mentioned earlier, the authorities are
setting target ranges aimed at reducing the proportion of dollar-linked and SELIC-indexed
debt issued. To strengthen the implementation of this risk reducing strategy the authorities
are already starting to  regularly issue Treasury Bills (LTNs), as a first step in the
development of an explicit, predictable and more general short- and medium-term domestic
debt issuance program. The implementation of such a general issuance program should lead
to the gradual establishment of more liquid benchmarks-and an associated yield curve. In
particular, the (plain-vanilla) Treasury Bill program could provide an anchor in the short end
of the yield curve, and would have to explicitly incorporate in its design such parameters as
target amount for the overall T-Bill program; desired maturities (e.g. 30, 90, 180 and 360
days); and amounts to be issued in each maturity; and appropriate changes in the calendar for
issues, in light of target benchmarks.
35.   Subsequently, and building on the intended development of a strategy for T-Bill
issuance, the authorities plan to gradually design a predictable issuance strategy for other
instruments which should enable the emergence of a longer-term yield curve. This issuance
strategy would provide clearer guidance to the Front Office and strengthen the Middle
Office's capacity to monitor deviations from its cost/risk targets.
108



36.    The design and speed of implementation of such a debt issuance strategy, by the
authorities, will have to take into account the previously mentioned constraints in terms of
macroeconomic conditions and market structure. The implementation of the strategy is
consequently of a medium-term nature. As debt duration is extended, complementary actions
related to money and derivative markets and to the structure of end investors will be needed,
so that the transfer of risk from the government debt portfolio to market participants can be
prudently managed.62
2.     Broadening and Diversifying the Investor Base
37.    The events of the turbulent last year highlighted the importance of complementing the
government's debt issuance strategy with efforts to simultaneously increase the ability of the
end institutional investors to measure and manage risk. In particular, last year's experience
pointed to the need to thetter delineate the types of funds and improve the disclosure by fund
administrators of the types of investment policies and products their funds can offer.
38.    The volatility in asset prices last year was magnified by the opening up of a credit
spread (desagio) for LFTs, the dominant security held by the DI funds, in the midst of the
implementation of mark-to-market regulations. The related increase in credit, market, and
liquidity risks facing funds (whose performance is measured against a deposit interest rate
index-the DI-derived from the derivatives markets) led to massive redemptions by fund
unit holders, forcing sales of securities into an illiquid secondary market. Lack of clarity in
valuation methodologies coupled with the implementation of mark-to-market rules
contributed to the contraction of liquidity in the government securities market as a whole. As
the crisis worsened with potential systemic risk implications, the Central Bank felt compelled
to undertake special debt exchange and buyback operations (as noted earlier) to permit the
orderly shortening of the duration of the asset portfolios of the funds. This contributed to the
inability of the authorities to meet their debt composition and duration targets. The legacy of
this episode is that it accentuated more hazard (i.e., the expectation by large funds and
proprietary desks of banks that the Central Bank will satisfy market demand for short duration
assets or in other contexts for hedges)
39.    The turbulent episode also highlighted how the lack of wider set of institutional and
retail investors (i.e., the concentration of buyers) can complicate debt rollover at times of
stress.63 The events of last year also demonstrated that some funds did not act in compliance
with the mark to market guidelines, while others provided preferential treatment to large
institutional investors in terms of disclosure (e.g., real time and daily reports of critical
information), while delaying disclosure and reporting to the small, retail unit holders. This
62 Experiences in other countries (e.g. Colombia) suggest that strategies that attempt to reduce refinancing risk
for the government, via issues of very long duration fixed income securities to pnvate market participants, can
run into complications if market participants have neither the capital nor the hedging instruments and
infrastructure to manage the duration gap or market risks they take.
63 Note that concentration of debt holdings in funds was in part promoted by the Brazilian tax system. The use
of exclusive funds by corporate Investors, high net worth individuals, and pension funds and insurance
companies became a way to avoid direct transactions in government debt, thereby avoiding the payment of the
debit tax (CPMF).
109



compromised the effectiveness of the mutual fund channel for the distribution of government
debt securities to retail buyers.
40.    The authorities have begun to take steps to rectify the problems highlighted by last
year's market distress. Already in 2002, changes were made in the capital markets law to
centralize all responsibilities for supervision and regulation of fixed income funds in the
Securities and Exchange Commission (CVM). This constitutes an improvement over the split
in such responsibilities with the Central Bank that had undermined consistency in regulatory
and supervisory treatment of funds.      To support this transfer of responsibilities, a
Memorandum of Understanding (MOU) has been signed between CVM and Central Bank,
designed to facilitate explicit resource sharing, including staff and information. The MOU
better defines the roles and responsibilities of the CVM and BCB in such areas as the onsite
and offsite supervision of funds, and creates a sound basis for the formulation of regulation
and supervision of fixed income funds (which largely invest in government securities).64
41.    To start to diversify distribution channels for government debt issues, the authorities
launched the Treasury direct program in January 2002. It allows retail investors to purchase
government securities at noncompetitive bids, directly from the government over the internet.
This program has not grown substantially, partly reflecting the limited diversity of existing
distribution channels. The diversification of distribution channels and broadening of the end-
investor base is not an easy task, given the concentration that exists in the institutional
investor markets and the lack of incentives for existing financial conglomerates to offer a
product to their retail clients that competes with bank deposits and investment fund units.65
(In effect, a handful of financial conglomerates (banks and their related funds) hold over 50
percent of outstanding government domestic debt.). The authorities plan to explore a number
of actions to improve the effectiveness of the Treasury Direct system.
42.    In addition, the authorities will establish in the near term  an interagency team
(consisting for example of representatives from the BCB, STN, CVM and the Tax Authority,
with participation by SPC and SUSEP where needed). Among its responsibilities, this team
will design a strategy and action plan to develop the end-investor base (institutional and retail,
foreign and domestic) for government securities.
43.    This interagency team  will also provide support and information for the CVM     to
develop a new and more uniform regulatory framework applicable to fixed income funds.
Such framework will entail: (i) a better demarcation of the types of funds;66 (ii) a clear
specification of the rights and obligations of fund investors and managers; and (iii) improved
64 MoU signed between CVM and Bacen: Convenio que entre si celebram a Comissao de Valores Mobiliarios e
o Banco Central do Brasil, com vistas ao intercambio de informacoes e outras atividades correltates, Rio de
Janeiro, 5 de julho de 2002.
65 Typical commissions charged by DI funds to small unit holders are between 400 to 600 basis points of net
asset value-and this does not include performance fees also charged by some funds. Straight deposits and
savings products pay well below the SELIC (even on an after-tax basis).
66 In Brazil this will involve differentiating between money market mutual funds, mixed funds, index funds,
closed and open ended funds and funds with limited lock in, hedge funds, and funds of funds.
110



disclosure requirements, especially of the risks associated with individual fund investment
policies.
44.    Regulations relating to performance benchmarks for funds will also be reviewed.
Today there is a proliferation of benchmark indices and rates.67   Many   benchmarks are
viewed with suspicion in local fixed income markets, both because of the lack of liquidity in
cash markets and due to large concentration of some of the underlying cash securities in the
hands of a few financial conglomerates.68 Hence, the design of fewer benchmark reference
rates with integrity is of critical importance
45.    As regards the valuation of debt securities, current regulations69 leave too much
discretion to market participants. This leads to differences in valuation of like securities and
raises questions regarding the integrity and comparability of performance comparisons across
funds. - There is thus a need to establish a more common methodology when marking
government securities to market, including using specific models and guidelines where there
is a lack of prices determined through secondary market transactions. The authorities have
committed to assessing the methodology used for valuation of government securities.
46.    The current portfolio classification methodology for accounting purposes is in need of
revision. The rules for re-classification of investment assets between "mark to market", "hold
to maturity", and "available for sale" give too much discretion to Fund Managers. What is
needed is a more standardized approach across various forms of funds before reclassification
is allowed. Here as in the case of valuation a review of existing regulations will be
conducted, in light of international practice.
47.    Actions in the areas above can have an important impact on the demand for
government securities. In particular, properly designed reforms in these areas will make
investment fund indifferent between buying a indexed versus a nominal asset.
3.     Improving Secondary Market Liquidity
48.    Liquidity in the secondary market for government debt in Brazil is very limited,
despite the very large outstanding stock of debt. Virtually all liquidity is concentrated in the
repurchase agreement (repo) market and in the derivatives markets (DI futures, and currency
swaps). The government's agenda to improving secondary market liquidity includes the
following mutually reinforcing actions: (i) further consolidating, standardizing, and
simplifying the debt securities issued; (ii) improving the primary dealing system; (iii)
examining possible revisions in the tax treatment of financial contracts; (iv) enhancing the
67 The benchmarks and indices include the derivative curve, and such reference rates as the TR, TBF, TJLP,
SELIC rate, CDI (or DI when used in measuring performance of funds), and the ANBID rates for debentures
For detail on how each of these different rates is calculated see page 63 of the Securities Markets Technical
Annex of the Brazil FSAP of March 2003.
68 Today there is nothing akin to a true Brazilian LIBOR. Local banks actively use the repurchase agreement
market, or the CDI market, as the true gauge of the marginal cost of funding. The CDI's are actually perceived
by some market participants to be less subject to influence by the authorities than the repo market through repo-
based open markets operations.
69 See CVM instgructions 365, 375, and also BCB circular 3086.
111



infrastructure for the trading, clearing, and settlement of fixed income securities, as well as
fostering the establishment of a government securities lending facility; and (v) enhancing
market integrity.
49.   The Brazilian authorities have already taken some actions to address the lack of
market liquidity. They have, as mentioned, significantly reduced the number and type of debt
issues outstanding, through a strategy of swap operations, re-openings, and debt buy backs.
50.   More recently, in March 2003, they issued regulations defining a new two-tier primary
dealer system. The regulations mainly concerned new criteria for the selection of "ordinary"
primary dealers, as well as of a new category of dealers referred to as "specialists".7o
Specialists and primary dealers will be required to make bids for a certain percentage in each
auction and account for minimum percentages of trading in selected securities. However, the
performance in the secondary market will have a greater weight in the criteria to qu.alify as a
specialist, while the requirements to bid in primary markets will be lighter. In effect, neither
the specialists nor the primary dealers will be obligated to "make markets" in government
securities in the traditional sense (by having to stand ready to buy/sell government securities
for a pre-specified amount within a certain maximum spread). The performance of the
specialists is being monitored through special reports by the market makers to the STN and
Central Bank.
51.   To bolster the new primary dealer system, the authorities plan to issue regulations on
benefits and penalties for primary dealers and specialists, so that the system can become
operational in July/August 2003. The success of the system in increasing the liquidity in the
secondary market, however, will be intrinsically linked to the achievements made in
implementation of the above mentioned debt issuance strategy. Without successful
implementation of a T-bill program and, in the medium-term, the issuance of longer duration
benchmark securities, a vibrant secondary market is not expected to materialize.
52.   Although no comprehensive action is being taken in the area of financial service
taxation, the authorities have submitted to Congress a tax reform bill that will, among other
things, give the Executive Brach the flexibility and the discretion to reduce the debit tax
(CPMF) rate from 0.38 to 0.08 percent. The speed of implementing this action will be
contingent on finding deficit-neutral alternative measures. The debit tax, combined with other
explicit and implicit taxes (e.g. reserve requirements and forced investments), likely
undermines liquidity in the secondary market for government debt, to the extent that it
encourages liquidity to move to relatively less taxed contracts (e.g., repos and derivatives). If
the CPMF rate is actually lowered, there will be a greater incentive for market participants to
trade in the secondary market for government securities. However, even a lower tax rate can
continue to create problems for secondary market liquidity, if it is not applied uniformly
across financial contracts.
53.   The authorities are supportive of the BMF plan to re-introduce an electronic secondary
market trading system for government securities (SISBEX) during the second half of 2003.
70 See the BCB and STN joint decision resolution number 14 of March 19, 2003.
112



This system and the associated regulations issued by BMF and CVM, should contribute to
greater liquidity and transparency in government securities trading. This is likely to be
especially important for the success of the envisaged STN Treasury Bill program. The
exchange (BMF) will clear and settle these transactions. This will be an order driven system
and participants will be able to see the consolidated order book and will have the option to
trade blind (i.e., not reveal identity to counter parties, which is important to large
participants). In addition, SISBEX will allow for the automatic use of call auctions when
liquidity of trading is limited in a specific security. Finally the commitments of specialists
will be tracked and monitored in this electronic trading environment.
54.   The development by the BMF of a wholly private framework for borrowing and
lending of securities over the SISBEX system can also help to improve secondary market
liquidity This would allow swaps of government securities used as collateral by clearing
members across different clearing entities. The facility would be based on marking to market
of open positions, in a way that they can be consolidated with other cash and derivative
positions. The BMF intend to introduce this facility toward the end of 2003 or early in 2004.
55.   As regards market integrity, planned actions aim  at improving reporting and
surveillance of exchange and over-the-counter trading. This should lead to enhancing the
integrity of reported asset prices and improving the detection of infraction and enforcement of
penalties. In addition, regulations regarding disclosures by intermediaries (e.g. primary
dealers and specialists) and fund managers will be reviewed to assure that disclosures and
internal compliance practices are up to international standards. Finally, enforcement capacity
at the CVM in regard to surveillance, inspections, and enforcement in government fixed
income markets will be reviewed and strengthened, partly with the help of resources from
World Bank TA loans. These initiatives will strengthen the integrity of the secondary markets
for government bonds, thereby attracting a more diversified investor base. Especially for less
well informed investors creating trust in the market, its intermediaries, and fund managers is
essential to improving participation
113

ANNEX 8: THE BRAZILIAN TAX REFORM PROCESS
A. Introduction
1.     This annex discusses Brazil's tax system   and analyzes the key details of the reform
package just presented to Congress. The report is divided into three sections. Section B
describes the evolution of the Brazilian tax system, providing insights into the reasons for the
low quality of the current system. Section C looks at the main obstacles hindering the approval
and implementation of broad tax reform and identifies the common elements of the different
proposals discussed in recent years, many of which have been incorporated into the Tax Reform
package that the new administration presented on April 30th 2003. Section D describes the Tax
Reform proposal sent by the President Lula administration to the Congress. And finally, section
E examines some administrative aspects of the Brazilian tax system.
B. The Brazilian Tax System
2.     During the last decade, the quality of the Brazilian tax system has suffered a continuous
deterioration. This is attributable to three factors: i) the necessity to increase revenues to attain
fiscal equilibrium in the context of a high degree of budget rigidity; ii) the incentives generated
by the system of revenue-sharing among the different levels of government; and iii) the fact that
the tax system has lacked the flexibility to handle the economic stabilization and openness
experienced by the Brazilian economy in the 1990s.
3.     The deterioration of the tax system can be characterized by: i) an increasing tax burden,
reaching 34% of GDP in 2002 - a level that is considerably higher than in other countries at a
similar stage of development; ii) the increasing share of the total tax burden accounted for by
cumulative or cascading indirect taxes (or taxes with an incidence on intermediate as well as
final turnover or sales),7' negatively affecting the competitiveness of the Brazilian productive
sector; iii) the regressive impact of the higher degree of indirect taxation; iv) the increasing
complexity of the system, imposing high administrative costs on taxpayers and the government,
and v) excessive fiscal competition among states and municipalities, resulting from the lack of
uniformity in national legislation on sub-national taxation.
4.     To understand the current Brazilian tax system, it is important to look back at the Tax
Reforms of 1967 and 1988. In 1967, the military government implemented a reform that
promoted a huge increase in revenue, as well as the modernization of the tax system. The
political situation at the time was conducive to a sweeping reform, allowing the rationalization of
the system based on the main principles of efficient taxation, and the introduction of key
technical guidelines in the Reform. The main innovations of the 1967 Reform were the
elimination of cumulative indirect taxes, the introduction of value-added taxation for federal and
71 An efficient indirect tax is one that fits one of the following descriptions: (i) it has an incidence only on final
consumption or on final sales, and not on intermediate turnover or sales of intermediate products in the production
process; or (ii) it has an incidence on the value added at each stage in the production process. (There is some
equivalence between the two because the value of final sales or consumption should correspond to the total value
added in the production process.) Value-added taxes are thus efficient indirect taxes, as are excise taxes on final
goods such as cigarettes, alcoholic drinks and automobiles. Indirect taxes that do not fit this description can be
defined as "cumulative" or "cascading" indirect taxes, because they imply that turnover or sales at several - i.e. two
or more - stages in the production process end up being taxed.
117



state governments, the simplification of the system via a reduction in the number of taxes, the
modernization of the income taxation system and administrative improvements that increased
efficiency in tax collection.
5.    The 1967 Reform also changed the assignment of tax instruments and the distribution of
tax revenues among the different levels of government. In line with principles of efficient
taxation in a federal system, key income and value-added taxes were assigned to the federal
government, which was obliged to share the ensuing revenues with the state and local
governments. The federal taxes that had to be shared with the lower-level governments were the
personal and corporate income tax (IRPF and IRPJ, or the Imposto de Renda de Pessoa Fisica
and Imposto de Renda de Pessoa Juridica, respectively,) and the federal value-added tax on
industrial products or IPI (Imposto sobre Produtos Industrializados). At the same time, the
Reform introduced the Imposto sobre ComercializaVdo de Mercadorias (ICM), a broad value-
added state tax on goods. Municipalities were assigned the Imposto Sobre Serviqos (ISS), a tax
on services, in the Reform. For both the ICM and ISS taxes, the rates were chosen by the federal
government.
6.    Figure 1 shows that the 1967 Reform had a strong and permanent effect on the tax
burden, which rose from 17% of GDP in 1964 to 25% of GDP in 1968, and stayed stable at
around this level until the mid-1980s. However, because of an increasing tendency to reduce the
taxation of selected sectors as part of an industrial development policy, as well as the
proliferation of fiscal incentives, total revenues from federal income and value-added taxes fell
towards the end of the 1970s. This trend was exacerbated by a fall in economic growth rates in
the early 1980s. At the same time, growing deficits were appearing in the national Social
Security and Social Assistance budget, since direct contributions to this system were not
matching payments from it.
Figure 1: Brazilian Tax Burden, 1960 - 2001

7.    As a result, the government created a number of new indirect and cumulative taxes, to
compensate for the loss in revenues from the federal income and value-added taxes (IRPF, IRPJ
and IPI), and to finance the deficits in the Social Security and Social Assistance budget. The
government created the PIS/PASEP (Contribuifao para Programa de IntegraqJo Social e
Programa para Formnaqao do Patrimonio do Servidor Publico) in 1983 and the Finsocial (Fundo
de Investimento Social) in 1985, which had incidence on the turnover of industrial firms, and
were cumulative (or cascading) in nature.  In 1988, the government created the CSLL
(Contribuifado sobre o Lucro Liquido), an additional income tax on corporate profits. (See Table
1 for a listing of the major taxes in the Brazilian system.) These new taxes had a burden that
varied considerably across different sectors and enterprises, leading to inefficiencies and large
distortions in relative prices, and negatively affecting the quality of the tax system. The result
was a deterioration in the competitiveness of the Brazilian productive sectors.
8.    The newly created taxes - especially the cascading PIS/PASEP and Finsocial - created
.significant distortions in relative prices for a number of reasons. First, they disproportionately
affected the industrial sectors (relative to the service and other sectors). Industrial sectors with
complex production processes had an especially high tax burden, since each stage in the
production process was taxed; the result was huge differentials in tax burdens across sectors.
The cascading taxes also encouraged the use of inefficient combinations of inputs, and increased
the cost of capital goods. They promoted vertical integration and industrial concentration (since
these reduced overall tax burdens for companies) which can hamper competition in the economy,
and discouraged the efficient outsourcing of non-core business activities. Given the cumulative
nature of these taxes and the fact that they are not levied on imports, they gave rise to an
excessive reliance on imports rather than domestically produced goods, to a degree that differed
across sectors because some sectors depended more on imports than others. Finally, the
imposition of taxation at each stage in the production process reduced the competitiveness of
Brazilian exports.
9.    Thus, the re-introduction of the cumulative taxes represented a backward step in the
process of modernization and rationalization of the Brazilian tax system. However, given that
these taxes had relatively low rates, and because various trade policy instruments were in place
that protected domestic industries, the negative effect of these distortionary taxes was not
apparent at the time. Also, the increasingly complex nature of the tax system made it difficult to
determine the extent to which the system had a negative impact on overall economic efficiency.
In addition to the complexities already discussed above, certain widely consumed goods began to
be exempted from the federal value-added tax (IPI).
10.   Unfortunately, the Reform of 1988 did not halt the process of deterioration in the quality
of the tax system, but it did result in an unexpected increase in the economy's total tax burden.
One of the main principles of the new Constitution of 1988 was that various government
functions such as education and health provision should be decentralized to lower levels of
government, implying a decentralization of government expenditures as well as tax revenues.
Thus, the 1988 Tax Reform changed the distribution of fiscal revenues among the three levels of
government in favor of states and municipalities. The 1967 Tax Reform had established that
35% of the revenues from the personal and corporate income tax (IRPF and IRPJ) and from the
federal value-added tax (IPI) had to be transferred to states and municipalities. Under the 1988
Reform, the share to be allocated to states and municipalities rose to 47%.
119



11.   But in practice, decentralization of expenditure responsibilities imposed by the new
Constitution was not implemented as quickly or to the same degree as decentralization of
revenues, generating a disequilibrium at the federal level. Even more dramatic, the 1988
Constitution expanded the social responsibilities of the state, guaranteed free access to social
services, established higher social security benefits, and guaranteed that all public-sector
employees would enjoy the same generous benefits that used to apply only to some categories of
public employees. In addition, the 1988 Constitution increased the degree of rigidity of public
sector budgets through the earmarking of a large portion of the fiscal revenues. All of this meant
that soon after 1988, the federal government found it necessary to find new ways of raising
additional revenue.
12.   To raise this additional revenue, the federal government turned to taxes other than the key
federal income and value-added taxes IRPF, IRPJ and IPI, so that it would not have to share the
additional funds with the states and municipalities. More precisely, the federal government
increased its revenues from cumulative indirect taxes such as the PIS/PASEP and Finsocial,
which - unlike the broad-based and efficient taxes IRPF, IRPJ and IPI - were distortionary and
of low quality. The federal government even created another indirect and low-quality tax, an
exotic tax on financial transactions called IPMF (Imposto Provis6rio sobre Movimentagao
Financeira); this was meant to finance - temporarily, for a two-year period - health
expenditures.
13.   Initially, these attempts to ensure that federal revenues kept pace with federal
expenditures worked. The main reason for this is that they occurred initially in a period of very
high inflation. The price indexation applied to revenues was higher than that applied to
expenditures; this, together with negative real interest rates that effectively made domestic
borrowing costless, meant that the federal government was able to generate primary fiscal
surpluses. Because of the high inflation, the government was able, in effect, to postpone a fiscal
crisis. Without the inflationary environment, the combination of a reduction in revenues,
increasing expenditure requirements, and a high degree of budget rigidity could have led to an
unsustainable path for fiscal accounts.
14.   In the second half of the 1990s, the Plano Real successfully brought inflation under
control, lowering it from 2,500% a year in 1994 to less than 4% annually in 1998. The end of
inflation defined a new fiscal regime in which it was no longer possible to control increases in
real expenditures through inflation. Despite the increase in the tax burden from 25% of GDP in
1993 to 30% of GDP in 1998, the fiscal situation gradually worsened. Fiscal reform was
nevertheless delayed; with the tight monetary policy of the period, public debt started to follow
an unsustainable path, threatening the stabilization process.
15.   In 1999, the Brazilian government implemented a rigorous contractionary policy that led
to a continuous improvement in the public sector primary fiscal balance, which (as a percentage
of GDP) was 3.25% in 1999, 3.5% in 2000, 3.7% in 2001, and 3.9% in 2002. These
improvements resulted mostly from revenue increases; this was necessarily so, given the
expenditure rigidities in the budget. The tax burden increased from 30% of GDP in 1998 to 34%
in 2002. As before, the federal revenue increases came not from greater use of the broad-based
and efficient federal taxes (IRPF, IRPJ and IPI), but from an increases in receipts from indirect
cascading taxes which did not need to be shared with the states and municipalities. In 1999, the
120



government increased rates of the following taxes: the indirect cascading taxes PIS/PASEP and
COFINS (formerly Finsocial), payroll taxes, CSLL and CPMF (Contribuicdo Provisoria sobre
Movimenta,co Financeira, formerly IPMF). The rate for the latter - the tax on financial
transactions - was raised from 0.10% to 0.38%.
16.   CPMF seems to have contributed to the large Brazilian bank spreads that have been
observed, and to some shift from bank deposits to investment funds. In addition, it appears to
have resulted in a decrease in the ratio of withdrawals to average balances, and in the migration
abroad of stock exchange transactions through the increase of American Depository Receipts.
On the positive side, it is worth mentioning that CPMF is being used as an important tool against
tax evasion, enabling the cross-checking of bank information and income tax declarations.
17.   Table 1 provides a listing of the major taxes in the current Brazilian system. Table 2
shows that the burden of indirect cascading taxes rose from 2.8% of GDP in 1990 to 6.3% of
GDP in 2001. The share of these indirect cascading taxes out of total revenue increased from
13% to 27%. These statistics illustrate the federal government's preference for revenue sources
that it does not need to share with the states and municipalities.

18.     On the other hand, the share of direct taxes and indirect taxes that are not cumulative (or
cascading) fell from 3% of GDP in 1990 to 1.7% of GDP in 2001, showing the reduced desire by
the federal government to collect taxes that it has to share with the states and municipalities.
Direct taxes maintained their share of about 26% of total revenue due to the increases in revenue
from the CSLL. Finally, the burden of payroll taxes increased from 7% of GDP to 8.5% of GDP.
19.     The increasing burden of indirect cascading taxes and contributions amplified the
distortions already introduced into the tax system before the 1988 Tax Reform. However, the
gradual increase in openness of the economy aggravated the problem, as the tax system imposed
high costs on domestic goods and production, relative to imports; these could not be
compensated for by trade policies. As imports are spared from many of the cumulative taxes in
the system, and since tariffs were significantly reduced, the negative effects of the tax system
became more evident, resulting in a broad demand for tax reform.

20.    The increasing share of indirect cascading taxes in total revenue also introduced a
regressive bias in the system, as the incidence of these taxes is on various forms of consumption;
consumption typically accounts for a higher portion of the expenditure of low-income groups
than high-income groups. An IMF study on the distributional effects of the Brazilian tax system
reveals that the overall federal indirect tax system is regressive; the PIS/PASEP and COFINS
taxes are especially bad in this regard, because their incidence on essential goods such as food
and medicine is significant. Because of the heavy concentration of the federal value-added tax
(IPI) on a few groups of non-essential products such as tobacco, alcoholic beverages and cars,
this tax is relatively less regressive.
21.    There are also significant difficulties with the state tax system. As a result of the new
Constitution and Reform in 1988, changes were introduced to the state value-added tax ICM and
the municipal service tax ISS, as follows: (i) ICM was broadened to include services, and
renamed Imposto sobre Comercializa,do de Mercadorias e Servigos (ICMS); and (ii) states and
municipalities were granted autonomy over the rates of the ICMS and ISS taxes, which had
previously been determined by the central government. Considerable differences arose between
the ICMS and ISS rates in different states and municipalities, introducing complexities in the
system. The administration of the value-added tax ICMS became difficult, because production
in any one state often involves inputs made in other states. At the same time, the interaction with
federal indirect taxes introduced cumulative characteristics in the ICMS tax.   All of this
amounted to additional distortions in the system with adverse effects on competitiveness in
foreign trade.
123



22.    At the municipal level, the tax on services (ISS) is clearly a cascading tax, since its
incidence falls not just on final services but also on intermediate services in the production chain.
The result is that competitiveness suffers in sectors where production is intensive in service
inputs.
23.    The lack of uniform legislation in the sub-national tax system also results in excessive
fiscal competition among states and among municipalities. In the case of the ICMS, each of
Brazil's 27 state legislatures has the authority to set rates independently of the others; state
governments thus tend to engage in fiscal "wars", offering excessive fiscal incentives in an
attempt to attract economic activity to their regions. The same is true at the municipal level,
where each municipality has the authority to set its own ISS tax rate. At the same time, to
compensate for the ensuing loss in revenue, states raised taxes and rates on tax bases that were
relatively immobile. In particular, high taxes were imposed on public utility services; the tax
rates on electricity and telecommunication services are more than 50% above the base charge in
most states. These high utility tax rates affect the competitiveness of the industrial sector, and
make the overall tax system more regressive.
24.    With regard to the direct taxation system, it is important to recognize its progressive
nature and the fact that Brazil has made important progress in the modernization of this system
since 1995. The main achievements were: (i) the tax base has been broadened, and the personal
income tax rate structure has been streamlined; (ii) the indexation mechanism previously used
has been eliminated; (iii) corporate interest income is now taxed, unlike before, resulting in the
more uniform taxation of the returns on financial assets; (iv) there are now more disincentives in
the system against transfer pricing; and (v) the taxation of micro enterprises has been simplified.
25.    The progressive nature of the basic rate structure of the personal income tax system is
evident from the fact that the threshold below which personal incomes are not taxes is high; it is
at least two times the median value of national personal incomes. On the other hand, the
generous deductions available to personal income taxpayers benefit mainly middle and higher
income earners, weakening the progressive nature of the personal income tax.
26.    Besides the corporate income tax, corporations must also pay the CSLL tax or
"contribution"; both of these are assessed on the same tax base. The result is that corporate
income is effectively taxed more heavily than personal income, affecting the competitiveness of
the Brazilian productive sector. This heavy corporate tax burden also creates an incentive for
companies to evade taxes, or to effectively remain in the informal sector of the economy. The
payroll tax is another tax that can encourage activity in the informal sector, at the expense of the
formal sector; it also raises labor costs.
27.    Finally, property taxes are traditionally under-exploited in the Brazilian tax system. With
the exception of large state capital cities, the urban property tax is not used by most Brazilian
local governments. Even less used is the federal rural property tax, which has a miniscule tax
burden of just 0.02% of GDP.
124



C. The Difficult Process of Tax Reform in the Nineties
28.   Since the 1988 Tax Reforrn did not address the main shortcomings of the tax system,
almost immediately after its implementation in 1989/90 there was widespread dissatisfaction
with the existing system, and increasing demands for another round of reforms. During the
presidential campaign of 1994, candidates had identified tax reform as a key priority for the
country in the new environment where economic stabilization was emphasized and the economy
was becoming increasingly open.
29.   In spite of public consensus over the necessity of tax reform and the electoral promises of
the new government, the Cardoso administration delayed the launching of the discussion and
negotiation process for the reform. This was due in part to the Mexican crisis at the beginning of
1995, which complicated the macroeconomic policies being implemented during the first year of
Cardoso's administration. Also, other constitutional reforms that facilitated the privatization of
public enterprises were considered more relevant initially, and dominated the reform agenda in
the Congress. The lack of a clear tax reform proposal can be considered another factor that
delayed the reform process. In 1996 and early 1997, as the government concentrated its efforts
on the proposed Constitutional amendment to allow the reelection of Cardoso as president, tax
reform was again postponed.
30.   In the meantime, the public outcry over the existing tax system grew louder, especially
from the ranks of the business community and the industrial worker unions, which - in the
context of the increasingly open economy and the exchange rate overvaluation - were negatively
affected by the pro-import and anti-export nature of the tax system. As a partial response to
these complaints, starting from 1996, exports and capital goods were exempted from the state
ICMS tax under Lei Complementar 87/96 (or Lei Kandir).
31.   Given the lack of leadership from the Cardoso administration on the subject of tax
reform, Congress and business organizations took the initiative. At the end of 1995, the
Chamber of Deputies created the Special Committee of Tax Reform in order to launch a process
of discussion on the topic with state governors and business organizations. It was only in late
1997, as the tax reform discussion process was taking steps in an undesirable direction, that
President Cardoso decided to play a more active role in the process. He initiated another round
of discussions on the topic, announcing that his administration was preparing a tax reform
proposal with the following general guidelines: i) simplification of the tax system; ii) elimination
of indirect cumulative taxation; and iii) revamping of the existing value-added taxation system.
32.   More specifically, the federal government proposed elimination of the PIS/PASEP and
COFINS cascading taxes and the introduction of a national value-added tax which would
incorporate the state value-added tax ICMS; there was some vagueness as to the precise nature of
this national value-added tax. At the same time, the IPI would be transformed into a federal
excise tax on goods and services, and the municipal ISS would be replaced by a municipal tax on
retail sales.
33.   However, the financial turmoil generated by the Asian crisis of late 1997 and the Russian
crisis of 1998 again resulted in the postponement of tax reform negotiations between the
Executive Branch and Congress.  In particular, the necessity of a rapid and deep fiscal
125



adjustment, which demanded an increase in revenue, imposed new and tough constraints on the
federal government; tax reform became even more difficult because of the uncertainty about the
effects it would have on total revenues. This uncertainty, and the fear that reform would reduce
revenues, persisted for some time after the financial crises of 1997/98. In March 1999, President
Cardoso said explicitly that tax reform was too complex and that the country was not ready for it,
announcing that the government would postpone discussion on the subject until the environment
became more conducive to reform.
34.   However, in October 1999, pressed by Congress and by the increasing public outcry over
the lack of reform, the government again made a proposal for tax reform, though this one was
somewhat different from the previous proposal.  The new proposal centered around the
"federalization" of the state value-added tax ICMS, i.e. a system whereby the federal government
would administer a broad federal value-added tax and share a fixed portion of the proceeds with
the states (through a new revenue-sharing mechanism), which would no longer have their own
value-added tax. At the. same time, the IPI would be transformed into a state (and not federal)
excise tax on goods and services; the municipal ISS would be replaced by a municipal retail sales
tax, as in the previous 1997 proposal.
35.   The reaction of Congress and state governors to this new proposal was very negative. In
particular, the notion of "federalizing" the state value-added tax ICMS was strongly rejected.
Congress instead took the original 1997 proposal of the federal government as the basis for the
new round of discussion, initially. But finally, in March 2000, the Special Committee for Tax
Reform of the Chamber of Deputies approved a proposal very similar in certain ways to the new
proposal made by the federal government in 1999. The Special Committee agreed with the
federal government proposal on the notion that certain taxes, in particular the cascading turnover
taxes PIS/PASEP and COFINS, needed to be eliminated. But the Committee did not agree with
the federalizing of the state value-added tax; instead, it proposed a broad dual value-added tax
(i.e. coexistence of separate federal and state value-added taxes) and a municipal retail sales tax
in lieu of the ISS. To compensate for the loss in revenue from the elimination of the cascading
turnover taxes, it was recognized in the debate that the rates of the broad dual value-added taxes
may have to be high, if its total revenue was to be maintained. A variant of the Special
Committee's original proposal, defended by the Rapporteur of the Commnittee, gained
importance in the debate. In this variant proposal, the loss of revenue from the elimination of the
cascading turnover taxes would be compensated for not by higher rates of the dual valued added
taxes, but by the introduction of a non-cumulative tax on goods and services. There was some
vagueness as to the precise nature of this additional tax.
36.   The federal government expressed its strong resistance to both these options suggested by
the Special Committee, and announced that the proposal would be sent to Congress for another
round of discussion. In August 2000, the government again submitted a new proposal, with the
following key features: (i) the cascading taxes would not be eliminated; and (ii) a new
destination-based value-added tax, administered by the states but under rules that were uniform
nationally, would be introduced. Again, the proposal was strongly rejected, especially by the
governors of the states that were net exporters, since these would lose revenue as a result of the
emphasis on destination-based rather than source-based value-added taxation. Also, Congress
considered it unacceptable for the federal cascading taxes to be maintained; it publicly declared
that the proposal was a nonstarter, as it failed to address the problem of cumulative taxation.
126



This declaration by Congress put the end point to the negotiations regarding this latest proposal
by the Executive.
37.   At the end of 2002, after intense negotiation, the Law 10.637 of December 30'h of 2002
was approved. It stated that the PIS/PASEP tax or "contribution" - originally a turnover tax -
was to be converted, in effect, into a value-added tax; this was to be done by allowing firms to
obtain a refund for taxes paid on the purchase of intermediate goods. To maintain total revenues
from PIS/PASEP, the rate for this tax was increased from 0.65% to 1.65%, with further rate
adjustment allowed in December 2003 if necessary to maintain revenue neutrality. The
provision is not yet fully in effect, and it is in any case temporary; the tax reform proposals under
discussion envisage permanent legal changes to be made to the tax system. Also, the scope of
the PIS/PASEP tax is small; it generates relatively low revenue, and many sectors receive
favorable treatment under it. Nevertheless, the Law 10637 can be seen as a step towards the
improvement of the overall tax system, albeit a small one.
38.   It is important to try and understand the difficulties encountered by the government in the
1990s in its attempts to reform the tax system, since this enables an appreciation of the main
obstacles that tax reform continues to have. The main reasons for the tax reform deadlock were
essentially: i) uncertainty regarding the effects on total revenues; ii) the dependence of tax
reform on a broader fiscal reform agenda, especially social security reform and the alleviation of
expenditure rigidity; and iii) the constraints imposed by the complex fiscal federalism structure.
39.   The necessity of maintaining the current level of total revenue, at least in the short run, is
the major constraint to the elimination of the cascading taxes. Simulations by Wemeck (2001)
and IMF (2001) show that if one substitutes these cascading taxes for value-added taxes, while
maintaining revenue neutrality, value-added taxes with rates of more than 30% on average would
have to be imposed; this is likely to be politically untenable in the current context of an already
high tax burden.
40.   The constraint that total revenues need to be maintained is likely to be less binding,
however, if other fiscal reforms are implemented. In particular, a reduction in the expenditure
rigidities in the budget would ease the pressure to maintain revenues. Thus, the depth of any tax
reform is likely to be highly dependent on the implementation of other fiscal reforms. Without
these other reforms, any tax reform would have to be conservative.
41.   Lastly, the political economy of Brazil's fiscal federalism and the strong regional
disparities in fiscal tax collection capacity limit any major changes in the sub-national taxation
system. All the proposals described above have implications for the distribution of revenues
among the different levels of government, and thus affect the control of lower levels of
government over fiscal resources. In this sense, all the proposals imply the necessity of altering
the system of intergovernmental revenue-sharing. Federalizing the state value-added tax would
be the best solution for improving the indirect taxation system. But this implies a loss of taxation
power by the states, and an increased dependence on states on transfers from the federal
government; this explains why states are strongly opposed to this proposal. The question of
whether value-added taxation should be origin-based or destination-based is another hotly
debated issue, since each of these forms of taxation benefits states differently, given that the
large regional inequalities in the country.
127



42.   Despite the difficulties discussed above, there is a growing consensus regarding the main
features of the Tax Reform. Table 3 compares the proposals presented since 1997. It is clear
that, in general, they share many common elements. The diagnosis as to what is wrong with the
indirect taxation system is common among all the proposals. Thus there is broad agreement on
the particular taxes that need to be eliminated. However, there is some disagreement on the new
taxes that should be introduced in their place. At least, however, all the proposals agree on the
notion that the value-added taxation system needs to be restructured and broadened.
43.   In addition, there is a general recognition that since revenue neutrality is a necessary
condition in the current context, the substitution of cascading taxes by value-added ones needs to
be done in a gradual manner. There is also general agreement that the CPMF should be retained,
since it helps reduce tax evasion; at the same time, there is concurrence that its rate needs to be
reduced gradually. Most proposals support the notion that there should be some uniformity in
the imposition of the ICMS tax (regarding its rate, for example), but that states should continue
to be responsible for its administration. On the same issue, the idea of destination-based ICMS
taxation is beginning to gain consensus in spite of the losses implied for the more developed
states, which are net exporters in interstate trade.
128



Table 3. Comparison of Main Features of Tax-Reform Proposals

D. The Tax Reform Proposal of April 30'h
44.    The Constitutional Amendment Proposal presented on last April 30'h includes the main
points of the consensus that has emerged in the last few years. In order of importance, the main
points can be summarized as:
*  Creation of national legislation requiring the ICMS to be imposed in a uniform manner
(with uniform rates applied) across states to avoid fiscal "wars", although states would
continue to administer the tax. The national legislation would have 5 rates and introduce
a progressivity bias with respect to the definition of these rates (lowest rate for goods of
the basic consumption basket);
129



o Establishment of the origin principle for the collection of ICMS on interstate trade, but
with sharing of revenues between origin and destination states preserved;
o Creation of a national fund for regional development, financed with 2% of the revenue
from the Income Tax (IRPF and IRPJ) and the Industrialized Goods Tax (IPI), for
allocation in the least developed regions of the country, as defined in law;
o Gradual transformation of the COFINS tax base from turnover to value-added (already
defined in the Law 10.637 of December 2002);
o Definition of CPMF as a permanent tax with rates varying from 0.38% to 0.08%;
o Partial replacement of the payroll tax - used to finance the social security system for
private workers - with a turnover or non cumulative revenue tax to be defined in law;
o Regulation of Federal Tax on Fortunes (IGF) by ordinary law in order to allow flexibility
to be used in the strengthening of its progressive nature;
o Increase in the progressive nature of the state inheritance tax (ITCD).
o Increase in the progressive nature of the municipal real state tax (ITBI-IV)
o Transfer of the federal tax on Rural Property (ITR) to the states, with rules regarding its
distribution to municipal governments to be defined by complementary law.
45.   The main objective of the Tax Reform proposal presented by the current administration is
to reduce distortionary taxation and increase the efficiency of the tax system. It is important,
however, to mention that the reform is in some sense a timid one. The main constraint for a
deeper tax reform is the uncertainty of its effects on revenue. Given the fiscal adjustment
requirements, any reform to be implemented has to be revenue neutral and this condition makes
a broader reform unlikely.
46.   In fact, the condition of revenue neutrality was preserved in the Tax Reform proposal
sent on April 30th . The Government guaranteed that the effect on revenue would be at least
neutral if not positive in the medium term. The potential reduction in revenues derived from the
substitution of a turnover by a value-added tax base will be compensated for by the broadening
of the tax base that would result from the gain in efficiency. In the short run, the government
will increase the rates on the transformed cumulative taxes. The Government has already
increased the rates of the new PIS - PASEP tax from 0.65% on turnover to 1.65% on value added
to compensate for the reduction in the tax base, and is considering further increases in order to
prevent revenue losses. For the COFINS transformation in 2004, the same procedure would be
applied: increase in rates on the new value-added tax. The reduction in CPMF rates to be
implemented by complementary law is conditional to finding alternative sources of revenue or to
reducing expenditure so as to maintain a given level of primary surplus.
47.   Regarding the state ICMS, it is expected that the end of the fiscal war would bring
additional revenue estimated at around 1% of GDP. For poorer states the government created a
130



compensation fund for the potential losses derived from the loss of use of the tax instrument to
attract investment to their jurisdictions.
48.    The Tax Reform proposal presented by the Lula administration has a progressive nature,
as seen from: (i) the reduction of cumulative indirect taxation; (ii) the requirement that the ICMS
has to have the lowest rate on basic consumption goods: and (iii) the introduction of
progressivity bias in the direct taxes on fortunes (IGF), on inheritance (ITCD) and on real state
transactions (ITBI).72
49.    A characteristic of the Tax Reform proposal is that it leaves many issues and definitions
to be -enacted later in complementary laws, rather than writing these into a Constitutional
amendment.    This is because of the potential conflict over the definition of details of
implementation (as in the case of the question of origin or destination base for the ICMS) and the
acknowledged necessity of using infra-constitutional mechanisms instead of having a very
detailed constitution that reduces the chance for future modifications.
50.    Without doubt, the ICMS harmonization and the reduction of cumulative taxes are by far
the most important pieces of the Tax Reform. They address the most relevant shortcomings of
the current tax system which are the multiplicity legislation of the state ICMS, resulting in a high
dispersion of rates and in fiscal war incentives, and the inefficiency resulting from cumulative
taxation.
51.    Another important point is the reduction of payroll taxes that would lead to the reduction
of labor cost, and thus to a larger segment of the labor market being in the formal sector. Finally,
the tax reform would introduce greater progressivity in the system of direct taxation; however,
the taxes affected in this regard are very small, and thus the increase in progressivity would also
be small.
52.    Finally, it is worth mentioning that the new political context favors the end of the
deadlock in tax reform that has persisted until now. The Workers Party (PT) administration is
showing that it is strongly committed to structural reforms. Tax reform and social security
reform are top priorities in the current reform agenda. On the political front, President Lula is
enjoying a period of strong popularity and legitimacy, allowing his administration to propose and
openly discuss far-reaching reforms. At the same time, the PT and its allies are building
consensus and support within Congress, the public, and civil society organizations for a broad
agenda of reforms in 2003. Recently, the Socio-Economic Council was established as a forum
for the discussion of the necessary structural reforms, and President Lula met with the 27 state
governors. All these developments, together with the broadening of the political coalition that
supports the administration in Congress, indicate that the political and social support needed for
the structural reforms are in place.
72 Many studies identified the indirect taxation system as mildly regressive. As the weight of indirect taxation will
be reduced by the elimination of cumulative taxes, it is expected the improvement on the progressivity of the
system. For more details on regressivity of the current tax system see Annex 8 on the Impact of Brazilian Fiscal
Adjustment on Poverty.
131



E. Tax Adnmnistration
53.   Although proposed reform of the tax system often focuses on improvements in the types
and mix of taxes used, it is also important to find ways to improve the tax collection system
itself, i.e. to engage in administrative tax reform. In a recent assessment of the Secretaria da
Receita Federal (Secretariat of Federal Revenue or SRF) in Brazil-which collects almost 70%
of federal tax revenues-it was noted that there are no studies of tax compliance in the country,
making it difficult to monitor and evaluate changes in the tax collection system (Casanegra,
2003). Notwithstanding this caveat, the assessment covered the strengths as well as weaknesses
of the tax collection system, offering recommendations for improvement. The main findings of
the assessment are summarized below.
El. Taxpayer Identification Numbers
54.   Individuals are assigned the same taxpayer identification number for federal as well as
state taxes. This simplifies the tax collection system and reduces the potential for tax evasion.
55.   Businesses, however, have different identification numbers for federal and state taxes; if
they operate in more than one state, they use a separate number for each state. This complicates
tax administration, and encourages tax evasion.
56.   The federal government is making efforts to convince the states to use the federal
taxpayer identification number at the state level. There are two possible ways of achieving this.
The first would be to work through the Sistema Integrado de Informa,oes Sobre Opera,oes
Estaduais com Mercadorias e Servi,os (SINWEGRA), a database being developed for the states;
the drawback here is that SINTEGRA has been under development for the last four years and
may not yet be ready for some time. Another alternative would be for the federal government to
reach agreement, one by one, with each individual state on the use of the federal identification
number for state tax purposes.
Eli. Other Administrative And Monitoring Aspects Regarding Collection and Payment
57.   Modern technology is widely used by SRF, with nearly 100%    of returns filed
electronically-a remarkable achievement which was implemented gradually over the last few
years. Tax payment is made through banks, an arrangement that works satisfactorily.
58.   Despite this advance in filing methods, monitoring of tax payments and liabilities
remains inadequate, facilitating tax evasion and delay in payments. As yet, there is no single
taxpayer account system, which would show each taxpayer's credits and debits for all Federal
taxes.
59.   To improve monitoring of tax payments and liabilities, businesses have been required
since 1997 to submit a quarterly return showing taxes paid in full, those paid partially, those
unpaid, amounts refunded by the SRF, etc. (This requirement does not apply to small and
medium enterprises subject to SIMPLES73 taxes.) But this system is only partially reliable due to
73 SIMPLES stands for Sistema Integrado de Pagamento de Impostos e Contribui,o3es das Microempresas e das
Empresas de Pequeno Porte
132



a variety of problems; to date, only information regarding the unpaid balances of 1999 has been
processed and sent to the Procuradoria Geral da Fazenda Nacional (PGFN, in charge of arrears
collection) for action. The establishment of a proper and fully functional single taxpayer account
system is of fundamental importance.
60.   It is recognized that it is key to monitor and control payments by large taxpayers; about
8,500 large taxpayers (out of about 75 million registered taxpayers in the country) account for
73% of collections. A number of attempts have been made in the last few years to establish a
special system for controlling large taxpayers' filing and payment requirements (e.g. the GRACO
system); unfortunately, these have not produced the desired results one. At present a new system
(SISCOMACO) is being implemented in some parts of the country to track large taxpayers; this
initiative is still, however, in its early stages.
EIJI. Collection of Arrears
61.   SRF differs from many other tax collection agencies in that arrears collection is handled
not by SRF but by PGFN, a separate agency (although both SRF and PGFN are a part of the
Ministry of Finance). It is not clear as to what is the extent of cooperation between the two
agencies, but it is clear that PGFN does not have the resources to deal effectively with arrears
cases. It can only handle 100,000 new cases per month, while at this time the SRF has about
700,000 pending cases. The total amount of arrears owed to the federal government at the time
of the SRF assessment, without including disputed payments and agreements to pay by
installments, was R$45 billion, or about 3.4% of GDP, which is not insignificant. PGFN will
need to be strengthened to prevent further delays in arrears collection.
62.   Other solutions to this arrears collection problem, aside from strengthening PGFN, could
be considered. For example, the collection of smaller debts could be privatized. In addition, a
change in focus in termrs of arrears collection may be warranted, to reflect the generally accepted
principle that collection efforts should focus on the largest and most recent accounts in arrears.
Currently, PGFN concentrates on the largest but not the most recent accounts in arrears.
EIV Tax Audit
63.   Progress in this area during the last few years includes the following: (i) selection of
cases for audit is now done using objective criteria; (ii) the largest 5000 taxpayers are given
priority (about 20% are audited each year); and (iii) taxpayers are notified in advance that a field
audit will take place, the tax years that will be covered and the names of the SRF auditors, all of
which can be confirmed through the Internet (reducing the scope for corruption).
64.   In spite of these advances, some important problems remain. The main one is the issue of
coverage. There are currently about 17 million individuals and 2.8 million businesses subject to
taxation. Yet during the year 2002, only 17,000 field audits were performed. Coverage of field
audits should be expanded, for both individuals and enterprises.
65.   At first sight, the additional taxes assessed as a result of the field audits appear large in
number; in 2002, they amounted to 29 billion Reals (or about 2.2% of GDP), an impressive
figure. However, only 1.7% of this amount was actually paid (or included in a plan for payment
133



by installments). The rest was either put to appeal or simply not paid. Serious consideration
should be given to this problem, and to the experience of countries that have addressed it. The
remedies include a reduction in penalties in the case of prompt payment, and the requirement of
a partial deposit prior to filing an appeal.
66.   Another issue warranting further analysis is the use of customs information for selecting
which returns are to be audited. It appears that record keeping regarding foreign trade is of good
quality; however, its role in selecting businesses for the audit of domestic taxes is very limited,
since many of these do not engage in foreign trade and thereby have no customs records. It is not
clear, therefore, how much use should be made of customs records for tax audit purposes.
EV. Other Issues
67.   Training of new and existing staff at SRF seems to be adequate, and salaries remain
competitive, although more so at the entry level than at higher levels in the organization.
However, if SRF is going to direct more resources towards activities such as tax audit, as
recommended above, it will need to hire more personnel trained in those areas (including
auditors), and also provide more of its own specialized training.
68.   A welcome recent development is the establishment of an internal inspection office
(Corregedoria-Geral) in 1997 to investigate cases of corruption and other unethical behavior in
SRF. The head of this unit reports directly to the head of SFR. Over the last 5 years, as a result
of this unit's activities, 161 SRF staff have been suspended or have received formal warnings,
and 109 have been fired. There is some concern, however, about a possible lack of resources
available to the unit in recent times especially regarding travel funds, which may hamper current
and future investigations.
69.   Administrative tax reform is important because it would increase tax compliance, which
not only leads to higher revenues but also to a more equitable tax system. It is important to
devote more resources and efforts towards improving the administrative tax system, even while
changes to the tax system itself-i.e. to rates, types of taxes, etc.- are discussed. The two, in
fact, would reinforce each other, because of the following: First, reducing the complexity in the
tax mix and increasing uniformity in rates would facilitate the control and monitoring of tax
collection. But at the same time, greater efficiency in tax collection would facilitate efficiency
improvements in the tax system itself.
References
Casanegra, Milka. 2003. "Brazil: Selected Issues of Federal Tax Administration." Report to
World Bank. Brasilia. April 20.
IMF. 2001. "The Brazilian Tax System." Report Mission. Fiscal Affairs Department.
Werneck, R. 2001. "Tax Reform in Brazil: Economic Achievements and Great Challenges."
Economic Department at Pontifical Catholic University of Rio de Janeiro, Brazil.

ANNEX 9: THE IMPACT OF BRAZILIAN FISCAL ADJUSTMENT ON POVERTY
A. Introduction
1.    Since 1999, the Brazilian government has undergone a phase of significant fiscal
adjustment, aimed at correcting the unsustainable path followed by public fiscal balances in the
nineties. The public sector primary balance rose from a deficit of -0.2 percent of GDP in 1998
to a surplus of 3.9 percent of GDP in 2002, a remarkable turnaround.
2.    This paper analyzes the effect of this fiscal adjustment on the poor. First, it examines the
evolution of poverty and social indicators over the adjustment period. Next, it assesses the
evidence on both the "direct" and "indirect" impact of adjustment on poverty. The "direct"
impact is evaluated by looking at the expenditures allocated to poverty reduction as well as the
incidence of the tax burden on the poor, before and during the adjustment process. The paper
also assesses the "indirect" impact of adjustment, or its effect on macroeconomic variables such
as unemployment, income, and prices, that in turn affect poverty levels.
3.    The main finding of the paper is that there is no evidence that fiscal adjustment had an
unduly negative effect on the poor. The analysis of the direct impact of fiscal adjustment on the
poor shows that public expenditures directed to the poor actually increased during the fiscal
adjustment period; it is, however, recognized that the poor were probably hurt by the increase in
regressive indirect taxation that accompanied the adjustment. Nevertheless, the paper presents
estimates indicating that the negative impact of the increased taxation was probably far
outweighed by the beneficial impact of the increased pro-poor public expenditure.
4.    The indirect impact of the adjustment is found to have been positive for the poor,
benefiting them through price stabilization and-in the longer run-4he stimulation of economic
growth. Perhaps most significantly, available data show that poverty-related indicators either
remained constant or improved over the adjustment period.
5.    The rest of the paper is organized as follows. Section B examines the evolution of
available poverty-related indicators over time. Sections C and D evaluate the direct impact of
adjustment on the poor; the former looks at public expenditures affecting the poor, while the
latter examines how the poor were affected by changes in the taxation system. Section E
analyzes the indirect impact of the adjustment on the poor, looking at how various changes in the
macroeconomic environment affected poverty. Finally, Section F summarizes the main findings
of the paper.
B. The Evolution of Poverty-Related Indicators Over the Adjustment Period
6.    Available data show that during the period of fiscal adjustment, poverty indicators have
remained practically constant. According to measures computed using annual nationwide
household survey data by the government research organization Instituto de Pesquisa Economica
Aplicada (IPEA), depicted in Figure 1, the percentage of the population below the poverty line
stabilized at about 33% over the period 1998 and 2001. Over the same period, the percentage of
the population below the extreme poverty line remained at around 14%. These indicators
135



remained stable despite the low growth rates, macroeconomic shocks and large changes in public
as well as external accounts that occurred during this period.
Figure 1: Brazil Poverty and Extreme Poverty, 1993 - 2001

7.    Basic education and health indicators improved significantly over the adjustment period.
For example, according to data from the Instituto Brasileiro de Geografla e Estatistica (IBGE),
the infant mortality rate fell from 31.9 in 1998 to 28.6 in 2001. IBGE data also tell us that the
net primary school enrolment rate75 went from 91% in 1998 to 96% in 2001-2002. Both these
indicators as well as others for education and health have continued their trend of steady
improvement over the years, even during the period of fiscal adjustment. This is probably due in
part to an increased emphasis placed by the government on education and health programs for
the poor during the adjustment period.
C. Fiscal Adjustment and Federal Expenditures Directed Towards the Poor
8.    To evaluate the impact of fiscal adjustment on public expenditures directed towards the
poor, we analyze the evolution over time of two variables: (i) overall expenditure on social
sectors and programs; and (ii) the extent to which these were well-targeted and truly pro-poor.
9.    In Brazil, fiscal adjustment implemented since 1998 has largely been based on increases
in tax revenues rather than on expenditure cuts. In fact, during the adjustment period, the
primary expenditure of the federal government actually increased from 16% of GDP in 1998 to
18% of GDP in 2001-02 - an increase in real terms of 4%-with tax revenues increasing by an
even larger amount to compensate.
10.   Expenditure allocations to many social areas are protected by constitutional or legal
provisions, and so overall social expenditures have not fallen during the adjustment period; on
the contrary, they have increased. Table 1 shows that federal social expenditures grew from
74 The 2001 figure is preliminary and may be revised.
75 This covers children between the ages 7 and 14.
136



15.5% of GDP in 1998 to 18% of GDP in 2001. Real federal social expenditures (evaluated at
2002 prices) increased from R$189 billion in 1998 to R$231 billion in 2001, an increase of 22%
percent overall or 5% annually. Thus, the increase in social expenditures exceeded that of
aggregate primary expenditures, with the former accounting for an increasing share of the federal
budget over the period of adjustment.2
